The Grundlehren der mathematischen Wissenschaften, Springer‚Äôs first book series 
in higher mathematics, was founded by Richard Courant in 1920 as a series of 
modern textbooks. Its objective was to lead students to current research questions 
through basic, comprehensive books. Today, it is often no longer possible to start 
from the basics and, in a single book, reach the frontiers of current research. As a 
result, volumes in the series have become increasingly specialised and advanced. 
The Text Editions of selected Grundlehren volumes have been specially adapted 
by their authors for use in graduate-level teaching and study. The most relevant 
chapters have been selected, rewritten for better comprehension, and exercises of 
varying difficulty have been added. Volumes in this series maintain a consistent 
level throughout, enabling students to build on their knowledge in preparation for 
research.

Preface
The purpose of the Springer series Grundlehren Text Editions is to publish textbooks
derived from Grundlehren books by their authors. They may serve as an introduction
to the subject of the corresponding volume. When I was asked to write such a
manuscript, I agreed because, being retired, I had the time and, moreover, because I
had already forgotten most of the details of our volume.
The present text consists of seven chapters, which in addition to parts of [24]
also contains some new subjects, which I think belong here. I tried to simplify the
presentation whenever possible and moreover corrected some errors and numerous
typos.
Each chapter contains several sections, all ending with a set of exercises. In fact,
there are more exercises than in the original volume, including among them some
easier ones which are recommended in particular for beginners.
Erlangen, April 2022
Herbert Lange

Introduction
The abelian varieties over the complex numbers is probably the most widely investi-
gated class of algebraic varieties. There are several reasons for this fact. Firstly, they
are closely related to theta functions, which pop up throughout mathematics and
even in physics. Secondly, to every smooth projective complete algebraic variety one
can associate several abelian varieties in a natural way (see Chapters 4 and 5) which
often help to study the original variety. Finally, abelian varieties occur in several
other mathematical branches such as number theory and integrable systems. So the
theory is interesting not only for algebraic geometers, but also for mathematicians in
related disciplines.
Apart from some additions which will be mentioned below, the present volume
consists of about half of the theory given in [24], namely that which may be consid-
ered the most important at the current time. However, ultimately the choice of topics
reflects my personal taste. Due to the textbook nature of this book, I have tried to
simplify proofs whenever possible.
Abelian varieties over the complex numbers are special complex tori, that is,
quotients of finite-dimensional complex vector spaces modulo a lattice of maximal
rank. Chapter 1 gives an introduction to the theory of complex tori and their line
bundles. The main point is that the sections of a line bundle can be interpreted as
theta functions on the underlying complex vector space.
In Chapter 2 abelian varieties are defined as algebraic complex tori, algebraic
meaning that the complex torus admits an embedding into some projective space.
Polarizations of an abelian variety are introduced and the decomposition of polarized
abelian varieties is investigated. Finally, some special results are given, such as the
dual polarization, the Pontryagin product and the endomorphism algebra of a simple
abelian variety.
Chapter 3 deals with moduli spaces of polarized abelian varieties. For this they are
given by elements of the Siegel upper half space, which implies that isomorphisms
can be described by actions of some symplectic group. The classical theta transfor-
mation formula implies that the moduli spaces with level structure can be embedded
into some projective space, which implies the same for most moduli spaces.
1

2
Introduction
Chapters 4 and 5 deal with examples of abelian varieties. To every smooth pro-
jective curve one can associate an abelian variety, its Jacobian. In Chapter 4 the
main results about them are proved. The theta divisor of a Jacobian is investigated,
a proof of Torelli‚Äôs Theorem given and a universal property of the Jacobian using
the Poincar√© bundle outlined. Finally, a proof of the criterion of Matsusaka‚ÄìRan for
a principally polarized abelian variety to be a product of Jacobians is given.
In Chapter 5 we study some other special abelian varieties. For abelian surfaces
one can say more about projective embeddings. To every smooth projective variety
one can associate two abelian varieties, its Albanese and Picard varieties, which are
both a generalization of the Jacobian varieties. To every √©tale double cover of smooth
projective curves one can associate its Prym variety. Finally the last section deals
with Intermediate Jacobians.
Chapters 6 and 7 contain two more special topics. In Chapter 6 the Fourier‚Äì
Mukai transform for certain sheaves and the Fourier transform on the Chow and
cohomology rings of an abelian variety are studied. This implies some results on the
Chow ring of an abelian variety. Finally, Chapter 7 contains an introduction to the
Hodge conjecture for abelian varieties. The Hodge group is introduced and proofs
of the conjecture in the two easiest cases of general polarized abelian varieties and
general Jacobians are given.
Each chapter is divided into sections and each section into subsections. The
last subsection of each section contains exercises of varying degrees of difficulty
and sometimes also quotes important results which should encourage the reader to
further studies. The first exercises are in general very easy and should be worked
out in particular by beginners. They are often just the proof of an equation or a
check of the commutativity of a diagram, which in both cases helps to understand
the definitions. The later exercises are often just standard results, included in order
to both challenge the reader as well as broaden their knowledge.
As for the prerequisites, we use the basic language of algebraic geometry and
complex analysis. Since abelian varieties are some of the most easily accessible
algebraic varieties, we do not need the whole theory. For example Chapters 1 to
6 of Griffiths‚ÄìHarris [55] or the first chapters of Hartshorne [61] in the algebraic
case are more than sufficient. Any other good introductory book (e.g. Harris [60]
or Shafarevich [124] for algebraic varieties and Wells [142] or Fischer [43] in
the analytic case) will also do. Whenever some deeper result is applied, a precise
reference is given.
The chapters can be read in the following order:
Chapter 1

Introduction
3
Sometimes one does not need the whole chapter to understand the next one. For
example one could read large parts of Chapter 5 right after Chapter 2.
Finally, a word on the intended audience of this book. According to the guidelines
of the series Grundlehren Text Editions it is mainly directed at graduate students who
want to learn the subject. By this I do not mean that the book is easy to read, certainly
for some parts one has to work. Besides students specializing algebraic geometry,
those of other branches, such as number theory and cryptography, mathematical
physics and integral systems, where abelian varieties play a role, will hopefully find
this book helpful.
It is a pleasure to acknowledge the help of some colleagues: First I thank Christina
Birkenhake for her work on the original Grundlehren volume, which went into this
manuscript. Moreover I thank Hans-Joachim Schmid for his support. Finally, I would
like to thank the referees and the editor for valuable suggestions.

Chapter 1
Line Bundles on Complex Tori
This first chapter deals with complex tori, that is quotients ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ of a finite-
dimensional complex vector space ùëâby a lattice Œõ of maximal rank. Complex
abelian varieties are special complex tori, so everything proved here is in particular
valid for abelian varieties.
In his fundamental paper [84] Lefschetz derived, among other results, the most
important topological properties of the first part of Section 1.1. In the second part
we explain the Hodge decomposition of the complex cohomology groups of ùëã.
The rest of this chapter deals with line bundles on complex tori. In Section 1.2
they are introduced and described by factors of automorphy, which are by definition
1-cocycles of the lattice Œõ with values in ùêª0(O‚àó
ùëã).
In Section 1.3 a proof of the Appell‚ÄìHumbert Theorem is given. It was first proved
by Humbert in [66] for dimension 2 applying a result of Appell [6] and by Lefschetz
in general in [84]. The present formulation is due to Weil [140] and Mumford [97]. It
describes each line bundle uniquely by its first Chern class, which can be considered
as a hermitian form on ùëâ, and a character on Œõ with values in the circle group C1.
In Section 1.4 the dual complex torus bùëãof ùëãis introduced. This allows us to define
the Poincar√© bundle P on ùëã√ó bùëãand its universal property.
In Section 1.4 the global sections of a line bundle ùêøon ùëãare described by certain
theta functions on the vector space ùëâ, which allow us to compute the dimension
‚Ñé0(ùêø) explicitly in terms of ùëê1(ùêø). In the next section this is used to compute the
dimension of every cohomology group ‚Ñéùëñ(ùêø) by changing the complex structure of
ùëâand comparing it with ‚Ñé0 of a line bundle ùëÄon the new complex torus. In Section
1.7 finally the Riemann‚ÄìRoch Theorem for any line bundle on ùëãis derived by just
taking the alternating sum of the dimensions of its cohomology groups.

1 Line Bundles on Complex Tori
1.1 Complex Tori
1.1.1 Definition of Complex Tori
Let ùëâdenote a complex vector space of dimension ùëî. A lattice in ùëâis by definition
a discrete subgroup of maximal rank in ùëâ. It is a free abelian group of rank 2ùëî. A
lattice Œõ in ùëâacts in a natural way on the vector space ùëâand the quotient
ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ
is called a complex torus. It is easy to see that ùëãis a complex manifold of dimension
ùëî. A meromorphic function on Cùëî, periodic with respect to Œõ, may be considered
as a meromorphic function on ùëã. Conversely, every meromorphic function on ùëã
is of this form. An abelian variety is a complex torus admitting sufficiently many
meromorphic functions. Although the topic of this book is abelian varieties, the first
chapter deals more generally with arbitrary complex tori.
Remark 1.1.1 In the theory of linear algebraic groups there is the notion of a torus.
Such a torus is an affine group, whereas a complex torus is compact. So one has to
distinguish both notions. The affine tori do not occur in this book. See also Exercise
1.1.6 (6), where real tori are defined.
The addition in ùëâinduces the structure of an abelian complex Lie group on ùëã.
We write its group operation additively and call the map
ùúá: ùëã√ó ùëã‚Üíùëã,
(ùë•1, ùë•2) ‚Ü¶‚Üíùë•1 + ùë•2
the addition map of ùëã.
Lemma 1.1.2 Any connected compact complex Lie group ùëãof dimension ùëîis a
complex torus.
Proof Let ùëãdenote a connected compact complex Lie group of dimension ùëî. First
we claim that ùëãis abelian: For this consider the commutator map
Œ¶ : ùëã√ó ùëã‚Üíùëã,
(ùë•, ùë¶) ‚Ü¶‚Üíùë•ùë¶ùë•‚àí1ùë¶‚àí1
and let ùëàbe a coordinate neighbourhood of the unit element 1 in ùëã. For every ùë•‚ààùëã
there exist open neighbourhoods ùëâùë•of ùë•and ùëäùë•of 1 in ùëãwith Œ¶(ùëâùë•, ùëäùë•) ‚äÜùëà,
since Œ¶(ùë•, 1) = 1 ‚ààùëàand Œ¶ is continuous. As ùëãis compact, finitely many ùëâùë•cover
ùëã. Denoting by ùëäthe intersection of the corresponding finitely many open sets ùëäùë•,
we get Œ¶(ùëã, ùëä) ‚äÜùëà. This implies Œ¶(ùëã, ùëä) = 1, since holomorphic functions on
compact manifolds are constant and Œ¶(1, ùë¶) = 1 for every ùë¶‚ààùëä. As ùëäis open and
non-empty, this implies the assertion.

Let ùúã: ùëâ‚Üíùëãbe the universal covering map. The Lie group structure of ùëã
induces the structure of a simply connected complex Lie group on ùëâ, such that ùúãis
a homomorphism. Moreover ùëâis abelian, since ùëãis. Hence ùëâis isomorphic to the
vector space Cùëî(see Hochschild [63, Theorem 17.4.1]). Finally, the compactness of
ùëãimplies that ker ùúãis a lattice in ùëâ.

Lemma 1.1.3 Let ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ be a complex torus.
(a) The natural map ùúã: ùëâ‚Üíùëãmay be considered as the universal covering map.
(b) Œõ = Ker ùúãis canonically isomorphic to the first homology group ùêª1(ùëã, Z).
(c) the vector space ùëâmay be considered as the tangent space ùëá0ùëãof ùëãat 0. From
the Lie theoretical point of view, ùúã: ùëâ= ùëá0ùëã‚Üíùëãis just the exponential map.
Proof (a) is clear. As for (b), the kernel Œõ of ùúãis the fundamental group ùúã1(ùëã).
Since Œõ is abelian, ùúã1(ùëã) is canonically isomorphic to ùêª1(ùëã, Z).
Finally, the complex torus ùëãis locally isomorphic toùëâ. Sinceùëá0ùëâcan be identified
with ùëâin a natural way, this implies the first assertion and then also the second
assertion of (c).
‚ñ°
Example 1.1.4 ùëî= dimùëâ= 1.
Choosing a basis of ùëâ, we may identify ùëâwith the field of complex numbers C.
A lattice in C is generated by two complex numbers ùúÜ1 and ùúÜ2 which are linearly
independent over R. So we have the following picture:
Identifying opposite sides of the parallelogram 0, ùúÜ1, ùúÜ1 + ùúÜ2, ùúÜ2, we obtain the
complex torus ùëã. The images of the lines 0ùúÜ1 and 0ùúÜ2 are cycles on ùëã, also denoted
by ùúÜ1, and ùúÜ2. Obviously ùúÜ1 and ùúÜ2 generate the group ùêª1(ùëã, Z). A 1-dimensional

1.1.2 Homomorphisms of Complex Tori
There are two distinguished types of holomorphic maps between complex tori,
namely homomorphisms and translations. First we will see that every holomorphic
map is a composition of one of each.

homomorphism of ùëãto ùëã‚Ä≤ is a holomorphic map ùëì: ùëã‚Üíùëã‚Ä≤, compatible with
the group structures. The translation by an element ùë•0 ‚ààùëãis defined to be the
holomorphic map

As an example consider the product ùëã√ó ùëã‚Ä≤ of the complex tori ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ and

rational) representation of these homomorphisms are just the projections and natural
embeddings of the corresponding vector spaces (respectively lattices).
Next we define a special class of homomorphisms of complex tori, the isogenies.
They will be of particular importance in the sequel. An isogeny of a complex torus
ùëãto a complex torus ùëã‚Ä≤ is by definition a surjective homomorphism ùëã‚Üíùëã‚Ä≤ with
finite kernel. The following lemma is easy to check.
Lemma 1.1.11 For a homomorphism ùëì: ùëã‚Üíùëã‚Ä≤ of complex tori the following
conditions are equivalent:
(i) ùëìis an isogeny;
(ii) ùëìis surjective and dim ùëã= dim ùëã‚Ä≤;
(iii) d ùëì|0 : ùëá0ùëã‚Üíùëá0ùëã‚Ä≤ is an isomorphism.
If Œì ‚äÜùëãis a finite subgroup, the quotient ùëã/Œì is a complex torus and the natural
projection ùëù: ùëã‚Üíùëã/Œì is an isogeny. To see this, note that ùúã‚àí1(Œì) ‚äÇùëâis a lattice
containing Œõ and ùëã/Œì = ùëâ/ùúã‚àí1(Œì). Conversely it is clear that up to isomorphisms
every isogeny is of this type.
The following proposition is an immediate consequence of Proposition 1.1.10.

Corollary 1.1.16
(a) Isogenies define an equivalence relation on the set of all complex tori.
(b) An element in End(ùëã) is an isogeny if and only if it is invertible in EndQ(ùëã).
Hence it makes sense to call two complex tori isogenous if there is an isogeny
between them.
1.1.3 Cohomology of Complex Tori
The aim of this section is to compute the singular cohomology groups of complex
tori ùêªùëõ(ùëã, Z) with values in Z. We only give the proof in the cases ùëõ= 1 and 2,
where they are particularly easy. For arbitrary ùëõconsider Exercise 1.1.6 (7).

1.1.4 The de Rham Theorem
The de Rham theorem states that integration of complex-valued C‚àû-forms induces
an isomorphism
ùêªùëõ(ùëã, C) ‚âÉùêªùëõ

1.1.5 The Hodge Decomposition

compute the cohomology groups ùêªùëõ(ùëã, C). Here we want to show that the complex
structure of ùëãyields a direct sum decomposition of these vector spaces, the Hodge
decomposition. We include only part of the proof. For a complete proof we refer to
[24]. But note that in the case of a complex torus it is considerably easier than for a
general compact K√§hler manifold (see [55]).

1.2 Line Bundles
In this section we introduce line bundles on a complex torus ùëã, compute its first
Chern class by means of a factor of automorphy and determine the N√©ron‚ÄìSeveri
group of ùëã.
1.2.1 Factors of Automorphy
Here it is explained how line bundles can be described by a factor of automorphy.
Before we define these factors formally, let us explain how they arise: Let ùëã=
ùëâ/Œõ be a complex torus. According to Liouville‚Äôs theorem there are no non-trivial
holomorphic functions which are invariant with respect to Œõ. However there are
interesting almost invariant functions ùúÉon ùëâ, meaning functions which satisfy an
equation
ùúÉ(ùë£+ ùúÜ) = ùëì(ùúÜ, ùë£)ùúÉ(ùë£).
The factor ùëìis classically called a factor of automorphy. Its defining property arises
by expanding the function ùúÉ((ùúÜ+ ùúá) + ùë£) = ùúÉ(ùúÜ+ (ùúá+ ùë£)) in two ways. It turns out
that ùëìsatisfies the relation
ùëì(ùúÜ+ ùúá, ùë£) = ùëì(ùúÜ, ùúá+ ùë£) ùëì(ùúá, ùë£).
(1.7)
So ùëìis just a 1-cocycle of Œõ with values in ùêª0(O‚àó
ùëâ). For historical reasons we call
them factors of automorphy or simply factors.
Under multiplication these 1-cocycles form an abelian group ùëç1(Œõ, ùêª0(O‚àó
ùëâ)).
The factors of the form
(ùúÜ, ùë£) ‚Ü¶‚Üí‚Ñé(ùúÜ+ ùë£)‚Ñé(ùë£)‚àí1
for some ‚Ñé
‚àà
ùêª0(O‚àó
ùëâ) are called coboundaries. They form the subgroup
ùêµ1(Œõ, ùêª0(O‚àó
ùëâ)) of ùëç1(ùëâ, ùêª0(O‚àó
ùëâ)). The cohomology group ùêª1(Œõ, ùêª0(O‚àó
ùëâ)) of Œõ

with values in ùêª0(O‚àó
ùëâ) is defined to be the quotient
ùêª1(Œõ, ùêª0(O‚àó
ùëâ)) = ùëç1(Œõ, ùêª0(O‚àó
ùëâ))/ùêµ1(Œõ, ùêª0(O‚àó
ùëâ)).
This is the cohomology group of the group Œõ with values in the multiplicative group
ùêª0(O‚àó
ùëâ) considered as a Œõ-module. The definition generalizes in an obvious way
to any group cohomology. Let us note only that equation 1.7 reads in multiplicative
form: ùëì(ùúÜùúá, ùë£) = ùëì(ùúÜ, ùúáùë£) ùëì(ùúá, ùë£).
In order to describe any line bundle on ùëãby a factor of automorphy, we need the
following lemma.
Lemma 1.2.1 Every holomorphic line bundle of a finite-dimensional complex vector
space ùëâis trivial.
Suppose ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ is a complex torus. As we saw in Section 1.1.3, the lattice Œõ can
be considered as the fundamental group ùúã1(ùëã) with respect to the base-point 0.

ùëâ) ‚Üíùêª2(ùëâ, Z).
But ùêª1(Oùëâ) = 0 by the ùúï-Poincar√© lemma (see Griffiths‚ÄìHarris [55, p. 46]), whereas
one knows from Algebraic Topology that ùêª2(ùëâ, Z) = 0. This implies the assertion.‚ñ°
Any factor ùëìin ùëç1(Œõ, ùêª0(O‚àó

Proposition 1.2.2 There is a canonical isomorphism

In particular, any line bundle ùêøon ùëãcan be described by a factor of automorphy.

1.2.2 The First Chern Class of a Line Bundle
For a complex torus ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ consider the exponential sequence 0 ‚ÜíZ ‚ÜíOùëã‚Üí
O‚àó
ùëã‚Üí1 and its long cohomology sequence
¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ ‚Üíùêª1(ùëã, Z) ‚Üíùêª1(ùëã, Oùëã) ‚Üíùêª1(ùëã, O‚àó
ùëã) ‚Üíùêª2(ùëã, Z) ‚Üí¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ .

ùëã) in ùêª2(ùëã, Z) is called the
first Chern class ùëê1(ùêø) of ùêø. According to Corollary 1.1.18 the groups ùêª2(ùëã, Z)
and Alt2(Œõ, Z) are canonically isomorphic. The following theorem shows how to
compute ùëê1(ùêø) in terms of a factor of automorphy ùëì: Œõ √óùëâ‚ÜíC‚àó. Note that ùëìcan

Theorem 1.2.4 There is a canonical isomorphism ùêª2(ùëã, Z) ‚ÜíAlt2(Œõ, Z) which
maps the first Chern class ùëê1(ùêø) of a line bundle ùêøon ùëãwith factor of automorphy
ùëì= e(2ùúãùëñùëî) to the alternating form
ùê∏ùêø(ùúÜ, ùúá) := ùëî(ùúá, ùë£+ ùúÜ) + ùëî(ùúÜ, ùë£) ‚àíùëî(ùúÜ, ùë£+ ùúá) ‚àíùëî(ùúá, ùë£)
for all ùúÜ, ùúá‚ààŒõ and ùë£‚ààùëâ.

1.3 The Appell‚ÄìHumbert Theorem and Canonical Factors
In the last section we saw that any line bundle on a complex torus ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ can be
given by a factor of automorphy. In this section we show that there is a canonical
way to distinguish a factor in every class of ùêª1(Œõ, ùêª0(O‚àó
ùëâ)). As a consequence we
obtain the Appell‚ÄìHumbert Theorem.

1.3.3 Canonical Factors
Let ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ be a complex torus. The Appell‚ÄìHumbert Theorem tells us that there
is a canonical way to associate a factor of automorphy to any line bundle on ùëã. Let
ùêø‚ààPic(ùëã) with ùëê1(ùêø) = ùêªand a semicharacter ùúí: Œõ ‚ÜíC1 such that ùêø= ùêø(ùêª, ùúí).
The cocycle ùëéùêø= ùëéùêø(ùêª,ùúí) ‚ààùëç1(Œõ, ùêª0(O‚àó
ùëâ)) defined by
ùëéùêø(ùúÜ, ùë£) = ùúí(ùúÜ) e(ùúãùêª(ùë£, ùúÜ) + ùúã
2 ùêª(ùúÜ, ùúÜ))
for all (ùúÜ, ùë£) ‚ààŒõ √ó ùëâ
(1.12)
depends only on ùêø(given the lattice Œõ ‚äÇùëâ) and is therefore called the canonical
factor (of automorphy) of ùêø. In this section we compile some properties of line
bundles which are most conveniently proved with canonical factors.
According to Proposition 1.1.6 every holomorphic map between complex tori is
the composition of a homomorphism and a translation. We will study the behaviour
of line bundles in both cases separately.

1.4.3 The Seesaw Theorem
In this section we will prove the seesaw theorem, using some difficult results on
complex spaces. Here it would be much easier to prove the theorem in the algebraic
category, that is for abelian varieties, and in fact we will only use it in this setting.
In this case one can use the analogous theorems in Hartshorne [61, Chapter III,
Theorem 12.8 and Corollary 12.9].
Theorem 1.4.8 Let ùëãbe a complex torus, ùëça normal analytic space and L a line
bundle on ùëã√ó ùëç.
(a) The set ùëç0 := {ùëß‚ààùëç| L|ùëã√ó{ùëß} is trivial} is Zariski closed in ùëç.
(b) If ùëû: ùëã√ó ùëç0 ‚Üíùëç0 denotes the projection map, then there is a holomorphic line
bundle M on ùëç0 such that
Lùëã√óùëç0 ‚âÉùëû‚àóM.
Actually the theorem and its proof are valid for any compact complex manifold
ùëãand any reduced analytic space, but we do not need it in this generality.
Proof (a): A holomorphic line bundle N is trivial if and only if ‚Ñé0(N) > 0 and
‚Ñé0(N‚àí1) > 0. Consequently
ùëç0 =

ùëß‚ààùëç| ‚Ñé0(L|ùëã√ó{ùëß}) > 0
and
‚Ñé0(L‚àí1|ùëã√ó{ùëß}) > 0
	
and the closedness of ùëç0 follows from the semicontinuity Theorem (see Grauert‚Äì
Remmert [52, Theorem 10.5.4]).
(b): According to the base change theorem (see Grauert [51, page 2 (2)]) the sheaf
M := ùëû‚àó(L|ùëã√óùëç0)
is invertible on ùëç0 and the canonical base change homomorphism
ùúë(ùëß) : M(ùëß) ‚Üíùêª0(L|ùëã√ó{ùëß}) ‚âÉC
is an isomorphism for every ùëß‚ààùëç0. It remains to show that the canonical map

The following corollary is called the seesaw theorem or sometimes the seesaw

1.4.4 The Poincar√© Bundle

Two line bundles ùêø1 and ùêø2 on ùëãare called analytically equivalent if there is a

1.5 Theta Functions
1.5.1 Characteristics of Non-degenerate Line Bundles
The notion of characteristics played an important role in the classical theory of theta
functions (see for example Krazer‚Äôs book [77]). In this section we define character-
istics of non-degenerate line bundles on any complex torus.
Let ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ be a complex torus of dimension ùëîand ùêø‚ààPic(ùëã). Recall that
the first Chern class ùëê1(ùêø) can be considered as a hermitian form ùêªon ùëâwhose
imaginary part ùê∏= Im ùêªis an alternating form with integer values on Œõ.
According to the elementary divisor theorem (see Frobenius [46] or Bourbaki
[28, 5.1 Theorem 1]) there is a basis ùúÜ1, . . . , ùúÜùëî, ùúá1, . . . , ùúáùëîof Œõ, with respect to
which ùê∏is given by a matrix

a symplectic (or canonical) basis for ùêø(or ùê∏).
Recall that ùêø‚ààPic(ùëã) is non-degenerate if the form ùêªand thus ùê∏is non-

1.5.2 Classical Theta Functions
Let ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ be a complex torus with projection ùúã: ùëâ‚Üíùëã. According to Lemma
1.2.1 the pullback ùúã‚àóùêøof any line ùêøon ùëãis trivial. On the other hand, the lattice
Œõ acts naturally on ùúã‚àóùêø. Hence ùêª0(ùêø) is isomorphic to the subspace ùêª0(Oùëâ)Œõ of
sections which are invariant under this action. Clearly the isomorphism depends
on the choice of a factor of automorphy for ùêø. To be more precise, let ùëìbe a fac-
tor of automorphy for ùêø. Then ùêª0(ùêø) can be identified with the set of holomorphic

1.6 Cohomology of Line Bundles
Let again ùêøbe an arbitrary line bundle on the complex torus ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ. The aim of
this section is to compute ‚Ñéùëû(ùêø) for all ùëû. For this we prove the vanishing theorem
of Mumford‚ÄìKempf (see Kempf [75]). Its proof uses the theory of harmonic forms
with values in ùêø.
1.6.1 Harmonic Forms with Values in ùë≥
In this section we define the vector space of harmonic forms H ùëû(ùêø) with values in the
line bundle ùêøand explain (without complete proof) its relation with the cohomology
group ùêªùëû(ùêø). Moreover we prove some properties of these forms needed in the next
section.

1.6.2 The Vanishing Theorem
Our approach to the following theorem, called the vanishing theorem, was first given
independently by Deligne [36] and with a slightly different method by Umemura
[134].

Chapter 2
Abelian Varieties
An abelian variety is by definition a complex torus admitting a positive line bundle
or equivalently a projective embedding. The Riemann Relations are necessary and
sufficient conditions for a complex torus to be an abelian variety. They were intro-
duced by Riemann in the special case of a Jacobian variety of a curve. A general
statement was given by Poincar√© and Picard in [104] and Frobenius in [46], although
it was apparently known to Riemann and Weierstra√ü.
This chapter contains the main results on abelian varieties. In Section 2.1 we
define the polarization of an abelian variety as the first Chern class of a positive line
bundle ùêø. By a slight abuse of notation we denote a polarized abelian variety as a
pair (ùëã, ùêø). We prove the Theorem of Lefschetz which says that if ùêøis a positive
line bundle, the ùëõ-th power of ùêøis very ample for any ùëõ‚â•3. Finally, we give a proof
of the Riemann Relations.
The second section deals with the Decomposition Theorem, which says that a
polarized abelian variety (ùëã, ùêø) splits off all polarized abelian subvarieties associated
to irreducible fixed components of the linear system |ùêø|. Furthermore we show that
for a positive line bundle ùêøwithout a fixed component, already the second power
is very ample. For this a version of Bertini‚Äôs Theorem is proved as well as some
properties of the Gauss map.
Concerning the behaviour of the linear system of the second power of a line
bundle ùêø, it remains to consider the case of an irreducible principal polarization ùêø.
This is done in the third section. For this we use a symmetric line bundle ùêø, which
by definition is a line bundle invariant under the action of ‚àí1ùëã. Such a line bundle
is contained in any algebraic equivalence class. The Kummer variety ùêæùëãof ùëãis
defined as the quotient of ùëãby the action of ‚àí1ùëã. It is easy to see that the projective
map associated to ùêø2 factors via ùêæùëã. The main theorem is that the induced map on
ùêæùëãis a projective embedding.
Section 2.4 contains a proof of Poincar√©‚Äôs Reducibility Theorem, which says
that any abelian variety is isogenous to a product of simple abelian varieties, which
by definition do not contain any non-trivial abelian subvarieties. For the proof we
use a polarization ùêøon ùëã. It induces an anti-involution ‚Ä≤ on the endomorphism
algebra, called the Rosati (anti-) involution. Moreover, using ‚Ä≤, we associate to any
73

abelian subvariety ùëåof ùëãits norm-endomorphism ùëÅùëå(which is a generalization
of the norm map in number theory) and consequently a symmetric idempotent of
the endomorphism algebra of ùëã. The main result is that any polarization induces an
isomorphism of NSQ(ùëã) = NS(ùëã) ‚äóQ with the space of symmetric elements of
EndQ(ùëã).
The last three sections contain some more special results on abelian varieties.
In Section 2.5 we introduce the dual of a polarization, give a result on maps of
smooth varieties into an abelian variety, which implies that an abelian variety does
not contain any rational curves, and finally study the Pontryagin product on the
homology group ùêª‚Ä¢(ùëã, Z). Section 2.6 contains a proof of the classification of
endomorphism algebras of a simple abelian variety, due to Albert [3]. In the last
section we introduce the theta group of a line bundle ùêøand show that its commutator
map is a generalization of the Weil pairing on ùêæ(ùêø).
2.1 Algebraicity of Abelian Varieties
In this section we define abelian varieties as complex tori admitting a positive line
bundle and show that these varieties are exactly the algebraic complex tori; that is,
which admit an embedding into a projective space.
2.1.1 Polarized Abelian Varieties
Let ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ be a complex torus. Recall that a positive line bundle on ùëãis by
definition a line bundle on ùëãwhose first Chern class is a positive definite hermitian
form on ùëâ. A polarization on ùëãis by definition the first Chern class ùêª= ùëê1(ùêø)
of a positive line bundle ùêøon ùëã. By abuse of notation we sometimes consider the
line bundle ùêøitself as a polarization. The type of ùêø(see Section 1.5.1) is called the
type of the polarization. A polarization is called principal if it is of type (1, . . . , 1).
An abelian variety is by definition a complex torus ùëãadmitting a polarization
ùêª= ùëê1(ùêø). The pair (ùëã, ùêª) is called a polarized abelian variety. Again we often
write (ùëã, ùêø) instead of (ùëã, ùêª).
A polarization ùêøon ùëãdefines an isogeny ùúôùêø: ùëã‚Üíbùëã(see Section 1.4.2).
Conversely, according to Exercise 2.1.6 (2) a homomorphism ùúë: ùëã‚Üíbùëãis of the
form ùúôùêøfor some polarization ùêøon ùëãif and only if its analytic representation
ùëâ‚ÜíŒ© = HomC(ùëâ, C) is given by a positive definite hermitian form. Hence one
could define equivalently a polarization to be an isogeny ùëã‚Üíbùëãwith this property.
A homomorphism of polarized abelian varieties ùëì: (ùëå, ùëÄ) ‚Üí(ùëã, ùêø) is a ho-
momorphism of complex tori ùëì: ùëå‚Üíùëãsuch that ùëì‚àóùëê1(ùêø) = ùëê1(ùëÄ). According
to Proposition 1.4.12 this means that ùëì‚àóùêøand ùëÄare analytically equivalent. Note
that ùëìnecessarily has a finite kernel, since otherwise ùëì‚àóùêøwould be degenerate.

Let |ùêø| denote the complete linear system associated to the line bundle ùêø. A
divisor on ùëãis a linear combination of codimension-one subvarieties with integer

the codimension-one subspaces of the zero-set and the set where ùëìis not defined,
both with multiplicity. Two divisors ùê∑1, ùê∑2 are linear equivalent if they differ by the
divisor of a meromorphic function, in symbols

(iv) ùêª0(ùêø) ‚â†0 and (ùêøùëî) > 0.

(i) ùëãis an abelian variety;
(ii) ùëãadmits the structure of a projective variety;
(iii) ùëé(ùëã) = dim ùëã.

2.1.4 Algebraic Varieties and Complex Analytic Spaces
In the sequel we will deal exclusively with abelian varieties. Hence we can work
either in the analytic or the algebraic category. In Chapter 1 we had to work of
course in the analytic category. It seems more natural to consider abelian varieties as
algebraic varieties rather than analytic varieties and we will do this without further
notice.
In this section, for the convenience of the reader we compile without proof some
results about the interrelations between algebraic varieties over C and complex
analytic spaces, mainly due to Serre [122].
To any algebraic variety ùëãover the complex numbers one can associate a complex
analytic space ùëãhol in a natural way. This construction is functorial: for any morphism
ùëì: ùëç‚Üíùëãof algebraic varieties over C there is a natural morphism of complex
analytic spaces ùëìhol : ùëçhol ‚Üíùëãhol. Moreover, many of the important notions in both
categories are preserved under the functor ùëã‚Üíùëãhol. For example: an algebraic
variety ùëãis complete if and only if ùëãhol is compact, and ùëãis smooth if and only
if ùëãhol is a complex manifold. Conversely, a complex analytic space X is called
algebraic if there is an algebraic variety ùëãover C such that X ‚âÉùëãhol.
In both the algebraic and the complex analytic category one has the notion of a
vector bundle and its associated locally free sheaf. For our purposes it is convenient
not to distinguish between these objects: we always speak of vector bundles, even
if we mean the associated locally free sheaf. In particular, for a line bundle ùêøon a

82
2 Abelian Varieties
complex analytic space (respectively algebraic variety) ùëãwe denote by ùêªùëñ(ùëã, ùêø), or
ùêªùëñ(ùêø) if there is no ambiguity about the base space ùëã, the ùëñ-th cohomology group
of ùëãwith values in the locally free sheaf associated to ùêø.
With a similar construction as above one can associate to an algebraic vector
bundle (or more generally an algebraic coherent sheaf) ùêπon the algebraic variety
ùëãover C a holomorphic vector bundle (respectively a holomorphic coherent sheaf)
ùêπhol over the complex analytic space ùëãhol. Again this process is functorial.
Not every holomorphic vector bundle on an arbitrary algebraic complex analytic
space comes from an algebraic one. However in the case of complete algebraic
varieties we have the following comparison theorems due to Serre [122].
Theorem 2.1.14 Let ùëãbe a complete algebraic variety over C. For any holomorphic
vector bundle (respectively holomorphic coherent sheaf) F on ùëãhol there exists a
unique algebraic vector bundle (respectively algebraic coherent sheaf) ùêπsuch that
ùêπhol ‚âÉF . An analogous statement is true for any homomorphism ùëì: F ‚ÜíG of
holomorphic coherent sheaves over ùëãhol.
Theorem 2.1.15 Let ùëãbe a complete algebraic variety over C. For any coherent
sheaf ùêπon ùëãthe natural maps ùêªùëñ(ùëã, ùêπ) ‚Üíùêªùëñ(ùëãhol, ùêπhol), ùëñ‚ààZ, are isomorphisms
of C-vector spaces.
Thus in the case of a complete algebraic variety ùëãwe need not distinguish between
algebraic vector bundles on ùëãand holomorphic vector bundles on ùëãhol.
Finally, recall the following theorem (see Griffiths‚ÄìHarris [55]).
Theorem 2.1.16 (Theorem of Chow) Suppose ùëãis a complete algebraic variety
over C and Z a closed analytic subset of ùëãhol, then there is an algebraic subvariety
ùëçof ùëãsuch that ùëçhol ‚âÉZ.
We obtain from this,
Corollary 2.1.17 Suppose ùëãand ùëçare complete algebraic varieties over C and
f : Zhol ‚ÜíXhol is a holomorphic map. Then there is an algebraic map ùëì: ùëç‚Üíùëã
with ùëìhol = f.
As a consequence of Theorem 2.1.13, Theorems 2.1.14 to 2.1.16 and Corollary
2.1.17 apply to abelian varieties. In the proof of Theorem 2.1.13 we used already the
theorem of Chow, but note that we used it only for the projective space Pùëõ.
2.1.5 The Riemann Relations
Before we proceed, we work out in terms of period matrices what it means that a
complex torus is an abelian variety.
Let ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ be a complex torus of dimension ùëî. Choose bases ùëí1, . . . , ùëíùëîof
ùëâand ùúÜ1, . . . , ùúÜ2ùëîof Œõ and let Œ† be the corresponding period matrix. With respect to

2.2.1 The Decomposition Theorem

Proof (of Theorem 2.2.1) Define ùêøùëü= ùëÄ‚äóùëÅ1 ‚äó¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ ‚äóùëÅùëü‚àí1. According to the

ùêø‚âÉùêøùëü‚äóùëÅùëü
with
‚Ñé0(ùêø) = ‚Ñé0(ùêøùëü) ‚â•1.
Hence ùêøùëüdescends via the natural projection ùëùùêøùëü: ùëã‚Üíùëãùêøùëüto a positive line
bundle ùêøùëüon ùëãùêøùëü:= ùëã/ùêæ(ùêøùëü)0 such that ‚Ñé0(ùêøùëü) = ‚Ñé0(ùêøùëü). Denote by ùëûùêøùëüand ùëûùëÅùëü
the projections of ùëãùêøùëü√ó ùëãùëÅùëüonto its factors.
We claim that
(ùëùùêøùëü, ùëùùëÅùëü) : (ùëã, ùêø) ‚Üí(ùëãùêøùëü√ó ùëãùëÅùëü, ùëû‚àó
ùêøùëüùêøùëü‚äóùëû‚àó
ùëÅùëüùëÅùëü)
is an isomorphism of polarized abelian varieties.
Suppose we have proven this. Applying this argument to (ùëãùêøùëü, ùêøùëü) instead of
(ùëã, ùêø) we can split off the principal polarized abelian variety (ùëãùëÅùëü‚àí1, ùëÅùëü‚àí1) in the
same way. Repeating this process we finally obtain the asserted decomposition.
For the proof of the claim it suffices to show that (ùëùùêøùëü, ùëùùëÅùëü) is an isomorphism.
The kernel ùêæ(ùêøùëü)0 ‚à©ùêæ(ùëÅùëü)0 of (ùëùùêøùëü, ùëùùëÅùëü) is finite, since it is contained in the finite
group ùêæ(ùêø). If (ùëùùêøùëü, ùëùùëÅùëü) were not surjective, we could apply Lemma 2.2.4 to get
‚Ñé0(ùêø) ‚â•‚Ñé0(ùêøùëü) + ‚Ñé0(ùëÅùëü) = ‚Ñé0(ùêø) + 1,
a contradiction. Hence (ùëùùêøùëü, ùëùùëÅùëü) is an isogeny. By definition we have
ùêø= (ùëùùêøùëü, ùëùùëÅùëü)‚àó(ùëû‚àó
ùêøùëüùêøùëü‚äóùëû‚àó
ùëÅùëüùëÅùëü).
Applying Riemann‚ÄìRoch, Corollary 1.7.6 and the K√ºnneth formula we get

deg(ùëùùêøùëü, ùëùùëÅùëü) (ùêøùëî) =  (ùëû‚àó
ùêøùëüùêøùëü‚äóùëû‚àó
ùëÅùëüùëÅùëü)ùëî = ùëî! ‚Ñé0(ùëû‚àó

2.2.2 Bertini‚Äôs Theorem for Abelian Varieties
The general Bertini‚Äôs theorem (see Hartshorne [61, Theorem II, 8.18]) says that if
ùêø‚ààPic(ùëã) is very ample on a smooth projective variety ùëã, then a general member
of the linear system |ùêø| is smooth. If in addition dim ùëã‚â•2, then a general member
of |ùêø| is irreducible.
We show in this section that in the case of an abelian variety ùëãthe irreducibility
statement is true more generally, in fact for any positive line bundle ùêøon ùëãof
dimension ‚â•2 without fixed components, even if dim ùúëùêø(ùëã) = 1. We need the
following theorem for the investigation of ùúëùêø2 in Section 2.2.4 below.

2.3 Symmetric Line Bundles and Kummer Varieties
As we saw in the last section, for the investigation of the map ùúëùêø2 it suffices to consider
the cases where ùêøhas no fixed components and where ùêøis an irreducible principal
polarization. We treated the first case in Theorem 2.2.8. For the latter case it turns
out to be convenient to take a particular line bundle in its algebraic equivalence class.
We need some preliminaries. First recall the definition of algebraic equivalence.
2.3.1 Algebraic Equivalence of Line Bundles
Two line bundles ùêø1 and ùêø2 on an abelian variety ùëãare called algebraically equiva-
lent if there is a connected algebraic variety ùëá, a line bundle L on ùëã√ó ùëáand points
ùë°1, ùë°2 ‚ààùëásuch that
L|ùëã√ó{ùë°ùëñ} ‚âÉùêøùëñ
for
ùëñ= 1 and 2.
Note that the definition is analogous to the definition of analytic equivalence of
line bundles (see Section 1.4.4), only the parameter space ùëáhas to be algebraic.
In particular algebraically equivalent line bundles are analytically equivalent. So
Corollary 1.4.13 immediately gives the following lemma.
Lemma 2.3.1 Let ùêø1, ùêø2 ‚ààPic(ùëã) be on an abelian variety ùëãwith ùêø1 non-
degenerate. Then ùêø1 and ùêø2 are algebraically equivalent if and only if there is
a point ùë•‚ààùëãsuch that ùêø2 ‚âÉùë°‚àó
ùë•ùêø1.
The most important case is when ùêø1 is ample, since then ‚Ñé0(ùêø1) > 0 so that the
(meromorphic) map ùúëùêø1 : ùëã‚ÜíPùëõgiven by ùêª0(ùêø1) (see Section 2.1.1) exists. Then
we have to compare ùúëùêø1 and ùúëùë°‚àóùë•ùêø1. In fact, the next lemma follows immediately from
the definitions.

2.3.3 The Weil Form on ùëø2
Let ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ be an abelian variety of dimension ùëî. Our next aim is to compute
the number of 2-division points ùë•‚ààùëã2 with even (respectively odd) multiplicity on
certain divisors on ùëã. For this we need a quadratic form on the Z/2Z-vector space
ùëã2, sometimes called the Weil form, which we introduce in this section.
For any ùêª‚ààNS(ùëã) define a map

eùêª(ùë£, ùë§) = e (ùúãùëñIm ùêª(2ùë£, 2ùë§)) .
This definition does not depend on the choice of the representatives ùë£of ùë£and ùë§of
ùë§in ùëâand eùêªtakes values in {¬±1}, since Im ùêª(Œõ √ó Œõ) ‚äÜZ. So eùêªis a symmetric
bilinear form on the 2ùëî-dimensional Z/2Z-vector space ùëã2. It is called the Weil

2.3.4 Symmetric Divisors
Let ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ be an abelian variety of dimension ùëî. A divisor ùê∑on ùëãis called
symmetric if (‚àí1)‚àó
ùëãùê∑= ùê∑. The main result of this section is Proposition 2.3.15,
where we compute the number of 2-division points, at which a symmetric divisor
has even or odd multiplicity.
Let ùê∑be a symmetric divisor on ùëã. Certainly the line bundle ùêø= Oùëã(ùê∑) is also
symmetric. Suppose ùêøis ample, so that the linear system |ùêø| is non-empty. Clearly
the divisors ùê∑in |ùêø| correspond one to one to canonical theta functions ùúófor ùêø
modulo C‚àóvia ùúã‚àóùê∑= (ùúó). In order to determine the theta functions corresponding
to symmetric divisors, we observe that, if ùúóis an even or odd theta function, the
corresponding divisor ùê∑is symmetric. The following lemma shows that the converse
is also true.

2.3.5 Quotients of Algebraic Varieties
In this section let us recall a result about complex algebraic varieties, which we need
later in full generality. In the next section we need only the special case of an action
of a finite group. The result is in fact valid even as a result about quotients of complex
analytic spaces (see Cartan [30]).
Let ùê∫be a group acting as a group of isomorphisms on a complex algebraic variety
ùëã(with Euclidean topology). The quotient ùëã/ùê∫, endowed with the quotient topology,
admits the structure of a ringed space in a natural way: denote by ùúã: ùëã‚Üíùëã/ùê∫the
canonical projection. Then by definition Oùëã/ùê∫(ùëà), for ùëà‚äÜùëã/ùê∫open, is the set of
functions ùëì: ùëà‚ÜíC, for which ùëìùúãis an element of Oùëã(ùúã‚àí1ùëà).
Moreover, recall that the group ùê∫acts properly and discontinuously on ùëãif for
any pair of compact subsets ùêæ1, ùêæ2 of ùëãthe set {ùëî‚ààùê∫| ùëîùêæ1 ‚à©ùêæ2 ‚â†‚àÖ} is finite.
Then

2 Abelian Varieties
Theorem 2.3.17 Suppose ùëãis a complex quasiprojective algebraic variety and ùê∫
is a group, acting properly and discontinuously on ùëã. The quotient ùëã/ùê∫is also an
algebraic variety. Moreover, if ùëãis normal, so is ùëã/ùê∫.
In the special case when ùëãis smooth quasiprojective there is a criterion for the
quotient ùëã/ùê∫also to be smooth. Note that the action of ùê∫on ùëãis said to be free if
ùëîùë•= ùë•for some ùë•‚ààùëãand ùëî‚ààùê∫implies ùëî= 1ùê∫, in other words, if all stabilizers
of the action are trivial.
Corollary 2.3.18 Let ùëãbe smooth and suppose ùê∫is a group acting freely and
properly discontinuously on ùëã. Then ùëã/ùê∫is also smooth.
2.3.6 Kummer Varieties
As we saw at the beginning of Section 2.2.4, for the investigation of the map ùúëùêø2 it
suffices to consider the cases when ùêøhas no fixed components, which we treated in
Theorem 2.2.8, and when ùêøis an irreducible principal polarization. In this section
we study the latter case.
Let ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ be an abelian variety of dimension ùëî. According to the last section,
the quotient
ùêæùëã:= ùëã/(¬±1ùëã)
is an algebraic variety. It is called the Kummer variety associated to ùëã. Looking at
the action of (‚àí1ùëã) locally around a 2-division point, one immediately checks the
following lemma.
Lemma 2.3.19 The Kummer variety ùêæùëãis an algebraic variety of dimension ùëî,
smooth for ùëî= 1 and smooth apart from 22ùëîsingular points, the images of the
2-division points of ùëãunder the natural map ùëù: ùëã‚Üíùêæùëãfor ùëî‚â•2.
Let ùêø= ùêø(ùêª, ùúí) be an ample symmetric line bundle on ùëãdefining an irreducible
principal polarization. Since ùúí(Œõ) ‚äÜ{¬±1} by Lemma 2.3.3, the semicharacter ùúí2
of ùêø2 is identically equal to 1 on Œõ. This implies that ùêø2 is of characteristic 0
with respect to any decomposition. According to Corollary 2.3.9 all theta functions
in ùêª0(ùêø2) are even. Hence there is a map ùúì= ùúìùêø2 : ùêæùëã‚ÜíP2ùëî‚àí1 such that the

2.4 Poincar√©‚Äôs Complete Reducibility Theorem
113
2.4 Poincar√©‚Äôs Complete Reducibility Theorem
This section contains a proof of Poincar√©‚Äôs Complete Reducibility Theorem. For
this we need some preparations, namely the Rosati involution and the description
of abelian subvarieties in terms their norm-endomorphisms, both depending on a
polarization. In the corresponding three sections we prove a bit more than is actually
needed for the proof of Poincar√©‚Äôs theorem, which is proved in Section 2.4.4.
2.4.1 The Rosati Involution
Let ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ be an abelian variety of dimension ùëî. In Section 1.1.2 we introduced the
endomorphism algebra EndQ(ùëã) and its analytic and rational representations ùúåùëéand
ùúåùëü. In this section we show that every polarization on ùëãinduces an anti-involution,
called the Rosati involution, and a positive definite bilinear form on EndQ(ùëã).
Fix a polarization ùêøon ùëã. It induces an isogeny ùúôùêø: ùëã‚Üíbùëãdepending only
on the class of ùêøin NS(ùëã). The exponent ùëí(ùêø) of the finite group ùêæ(ùêø) = Ker ùúôùêø
is called the exponent of the polarization ùêø. According to Proposition 1.1.15 there
exists a unique isogeny ùúìùêø: bùëã‚Üíùëãsuch that
ùúìùêøùúôùêø= ùëí(ùêø)ùëã
and
ùúôùêøùúìùêø= ùëí(ùêø) bùëã,
the multiplications by the integer ùëí(ùêø) on ùëãand bùëãrespectively. Thus ùúôùêøhas an
inverse in HomQ( bùëã, ùëã), namely

The group of automorphisms of an abelian variety need not be finite. For an
example, see Exercise 1.1.6 (14).
Recall that an automophism of a polarized abelian variety (ùëã, ùêø) is an automor-

ùëê1( ùëì‚àóùêø) = ùëê1(ùêø). Clearly these automorphisms form a group.
Corollary 2.4.10 The group of automorphisms of any polarized abelian variety
(ùëã, ùêø) is finite.

2.4.2 Polarizations
Recall that by definition a polarization on an abelian variety ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ is the class of
an ample line bundle ùêøin NS(ùëã). By abuse of notation we often write ùêøinstead of its
class in NS(ùëã). If ùêøis of type (ùëë1, . . . , ùëëùëî), we define the degree of the polarization
ùêøto be the product ùëë= ùëë1 ¬∑ . . . ¬∑ ùëëùëî. In this section we study the subset of NS(ùëã)
of polarizations of a given degree. The aim is to give a formula for the number of
isomorphism classes of such polarizations.
Fix a polarization ùêø0 on ùëã. The inverse ùúô‚àí1
ùêø0 of ùúôùêø0 : ùëã‚Üíbùëãexists in HomQ( bùëã, ùëã).

depending only on the class of ùêøin NS(ùëã). Denoting NSQ(ùëã) = NS(ùëã) ‚äóZ Q, the
polarization ùêø0 induces in this way a homomorphism of abelian groups
NSQ(ùëã) ‚ÜíEndQ(ùëã),
ùêø‚Ü¶‚Üíùúô‚àí1

2.4.3 Abelian Subvarieties and Symmetric Idempotents
In this section we describe the set of abelian subvarieties of an abelian variety
ùëãin terms of the endomorphism algebra EndQ(ùëã). Given a polarization ùêøon ùëã
we associate to every abelian subvariety ùëåof ùëãan endomorphism ùëÅùëå, the norm-
endomorphism, and a symmetric idempotent ùúÄùëå. We will see that the symmetric
idempotents are in one to one correspondence to the abelian subvarieties of ùëã. This
leads to a criterion for an endomorphism to be a norm-endomorphism. One of the
various consequences is that EndQ(ùëã) is a semisimple Q-algebra.

are isogenies. So ùõΩ= bùúÑùëåùúôùêøùúÑùëç= 0. In the same way we obtain ùõæ= bùúÑùëçùúôùêøùúÑùëå= 0.
Finally we have ùõº= bùúÑùëåùúôùêøùúÑùëå= ùúôùúÑùëå‚àóùêøand similarly ùõø= ùúôùúÑùëç‚àóùêø. This this implies the
assertion.

varieties ùëãùúàand the integers ùëõùúàare uniquely determined up to isogenies and per-
mutations.

2.5 Some Special Results
In the first section we introduce the dual of a polarization of an abelian variety. The
second section contains a result on rational maps from a smooth projective variety
to an abelian variety, namely that it is defined everywhere. Finally, the Pontryagin
product, which was already introduced in Exercise 1.1.6 (11), is defined in a slightly
different way and investigated in more detail.
2.5.1 The Dual Polarization
Let (ùëã, ùêø) be a polarized abelian variety of dimension ùëî. In Sections 1.4.1 and 1.4.4
the dual abelian variety bùëãand the Poincar√© bundle P on ùëã√ó bùëãwere introduced. In
this section we show that the polarization ùêøinduces a polarization ùêøùõøon the dual
abelian variety bùëã, which can be considered as the dual polarization of ùêø, in the sense
that the double dual polarization coincides with the polarization ùêø. The reason why
we do not denote it by bùêøis that bùêøalready denotes the Fourier-Mukai transform of
ùêø. In Section 6.1.2 below we will define bùêøand see in Exercise 6.1.4 (7) how it is
related to ùêøùõø.

Recall that a rational map ùëì: ùëåd ùëãof smooth complex algebraic varieties is an

with ùë¶‚ààùëà.

that the induced map Oùëã,ùë•‚ÜíC(ùëå) factorizes via Oùëå,ùë¶. Note that then necessarily
ùë•= ùëì(ùë¶).
Theorem 2.5.9 Any rational map ùëì: ùëåd ùëãfrom a smooth variety ùëåto an abelian

Proof First recall that ùëìis not defined at most in a subvariety of codimension ‚â•2,

ùêπ(ùë¶1, ùë¶2) = ùëì(ùë¶1) ‚àíùëì(ùë¶2)

for all ùë¶‚ààùëâ; in particular ùëìis defined at ùë¶0. The converse implication is obvious.
Suppose now ùëìis not defined at a point ùë¶‚ààùëå. Then ùêπis not defined at (ùë¶, ùë¶). By
what we have said above, this means that the homomorphism ùúô: Oùëã,0 ‚ÜíC(ùëå√ó ùëå)

Im ùúô‚äÑOùëå√óùëå,(ùë¶,ùë¶).
(2.8)

Proposition 2.5.10 Every rational map ùëì: Pùëõd ùëãfrom projective space to an
abelian variety ùëãis constant.
In particular an abelian variety does not contain any rational curves.
Proof According to Theorem 2.5.9 the map ùëìis defined everywhere. Since any two
points in Pùëõare joined by a line P1, it suffices to show that any morphism ùëì: P1 ‚Üíùëã
is constant.
Let ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ and ùë£1, . . . , ùë£ùëîbe a basis of ùëâ. Then dùë£1, . . . , dùë£ùëîis a basis
of holomorphic differentials of ùëã. Since P1 does not admit any non-zero global
holomorphic differential, we have ùëì‚àódùë£ùúà= 0 for ùúà= 1, . . . , ùëî. Thus ùëìis constant.‚ñ°
2.5.3 The Pontryagin Product
Let ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ be an abelian variety of dimension ùëî. The addition on ùëãinduces
a multiplication on the homology ring ùêª‚Ä¢(ùëã, Z), the Pontryagin product. In this
section we show that the Pontryagin product is dual to the cup product in ùêª‚Ä¢(ùëã, Z).
As a first application we derive a formula which expresses the homology class of
an ample divisor by a symplectic basis of Œõ = ùêª1(ùëã, Z) in terms of the Pontryagin
product. The results of this section generalize immediately to any non-degenerate
line bundle on a complex torus.
Let √ó: ùêªùëù(ùëã, Z) √ó ùêªùëû(ùëã, Z) ‚Üíùêªùëù+ùëû(ùëã√ó ùëã, Z) denote the exterior homology
product. The addition map ùúá: ùëã√ó ùëã‚Üíùëãinduces a homomorphism ùúá‚àó: ùêªùëù+ùëû(ùëã√ó
ùëã, Z) ‚Üíùêªùëù+ùëû(ùëã, Z). The Pontryagin product on ùëãis defined to be the composition
‚òÖ: ùêªùëù(ùëã, Z) √ó ùêªùëû(ùëã, Z)
√ó
‚àí‚Üíùêªùëù+ùëû(ùëã√ó ùëã, Z)
ùúá‚àó
‚àí‚Üíùêªùëù+ùëû(ùëã, Z).
For the definition of the Pontryagin product in terms of cycles see Exercise 1.1.6
(11).
Lemma 2.5.11 The Pontryagin product is anti-commutative; that is,
ùúé‚òÖùúè= (‚àí1) ùëù+ùëûùúè‚òÖùúé
for all ùúé‚ààùêªùëù(ùëã, Z) and ùúè‚ààùêªùëû(ùëã, Z).
Proof This follows from the fact that the exterior homology product is anti-
commutative (see Greenberg‚ÄìHarper [53, Corollary 29.29]).
‚ñ°

Finally, let me say a few words on the role of abelian varieties in other subjects.
Abelian varieties and their theta functions occur in many other parts of mathematics
and theoretical physics. Let me mention only mirror symmetry in string theory. An
explanation of it in this introductory book would be too long. Too many things would
have to be explained.
But let me mention how abelian varieties arise in the theory of integrable systems.
Many integrable systems of classical mechanics admit a complexification of the phase
space and the time variable. The precise mathematical notion is that of an algebraic
complete integrable system introduced by Adler and van Moerbeke in [1]. This gives
many integrable systems which can be linearized on open sets of abelian varieties.

140
2 Abelian Varieties
To give a complete definition here together with motivation and some results would
lead us too far away. So we refer the interested reader to the book [2] by Adler, van
Moerbeke and Vanhaecke as well as the article [40] by Donagi and Markman.
2.6 The Endomorphism Algebra of a Simple Abelian Variety
Let ùëãbe a simple abelian variety of dimension ùëîand ùêøa polarization on ùëã.
According to Corollary 2.4.26 the algebra
ùêπ= EndQ(ùëã)
is a skew field of finite dimension over Q. The Rosati involution ùëì‚Ü¶‚Üíùëì‚Ä≤ with respect
to the polarization ùêøis an anti-involution on ùêπsuch that the map

section.

2.7 The Commutator Map Associated to a Theta Group
To every line bundle ùêøon an abelian variety one can associate a group, its theta
group. The main result of this section is that the commutator map of the theta group
of ùêøcoincides with the Weil pairing on ùêæ(ùêø).

2 Abelian Varieties

Chapter 3
Moduli Spaces
In this chapter several moduli spaces of polarized abelian varieties with an additional
structure are constructed. The first thing to notice is that the notion ‚Äúmoduli space‚Äù
is considered in a slightly naive way: A moduli space for a set of abelian varieties
with some additional structure means a complex analytic space whose points are in
some natural one to one correspondence with the elements of the set. In many case
we show that the spaces are manifolds or algebraic varieties. The uniqueness and the
functorial properties of these spaces will be totally ignored.
Given a type ùê∑= diag(ùëë1, . . . , ùëëùëî), there is a polarized abelian variety of this
type if and only if ùëëùëî> 0. If in this chapter a type occurs, this is always assumed.
The starting point for studying the corresponding moduli spaces is the Siegel
upper half space ‚Ñåùëîof complex symmetric (ùëî√ó ùëî)-matrices with positive definite
imaginary part. It parametrizes the set of polarized abelian varieties of type ùê∑with
a symplectic basis. The corresponding symplectic group ùê∫ùê∑acts on ‚Ñåùëîin a natural
way and the quotient Aùëî= ‚Ñåùëî/ùê∫ùê∑is a moduli space for polarized abelian varieties
of type ùê∑(Theorem 3.1.12). In Section 3.2 several level structures are introduced.
The corresponding moduli spaces are quotients of ‚Ñåùëîby the corresponding subgroup
of ùê∫ùê∑.
The last part of the chapter is devoted to Theorem 3.5.4, due to Igusa [70], which
provides an analytic embedding of the moduli space of polarized abelian varieties
Aùê∑(ùê∑)0 with orthogonal level ùê∑-structure into projective space. It is not difficult
to conclude that Aùê∑(ùê∑)0 and hence also Aùê∑are actually algebraic varieties.
Here is a short outline of the proof of Igusa‚Äôs Theorem: First a universal family
of abelian varieties Xùê∑‚Üí‚Ñåùëîparametrizing all polarized abelian varieties of type
ùê∑with symplectic basis is constructed. Since the classical factor of automorphy is
holomorphic in ùëç‚àà‚Ñåùëî, it extends to a factor on Xùê∑and this defines a line bundle L
on Xùê∑. Composing the zero section of Xùê∑‚Üí‚Ñåùëîwith the map Xùê∑‚ÜíPùëÅassociated
to a certain sublinear system of |L|, we get a holomorphic map ùúìùê∑: ‚Ñåùëî‚ÜíPùëÅ.
Now the classical Theta Transformation Formula 3.3.9 implies that ùúìùê∑factorizes
via the quotient Aùê∑(ùê∑)0 of ‚Ñåùëî, to give a holomorphic map ùúìùê∑: Aùê∑(ùê∑)0 ‚ÜíPùëÅ,
which turns out to be an embedding.

3 Moduli Spaces
Finally a word about period matrices: in almost every book they appear in different
forms. The following two requirements seem natural:
1. period matrices should be (ùëî√ó 2ùëî)-matrices rather that (2ùëî√ó ùëî)-matrices;
2. a symplectic

ùõæ

ùõø

‚ààSp2ùëî(R) should act on ‚Ñåùëîby ùëç‚Ü¶‚Üí(ùõºùëç+ ùõΩ)(ùõæùëç+ ùõø)‚àí1.
Under these conditions the period matrices are necessarily of the form (ùëç, ùê∑) with
ùëç‚àà‚Ñåùëîand type ùê∑. There is a slight disadvantage: there is a transposition coming
up. Namely, if ùëÖis the rational representation of an isomorphism of polarized abelian
varieties, the corresponding action on ‚Ñåùëîis given by ùë°ùëÖ.
3.1 The Moduli Spaces of Polarized Abelian Varieties
This section contains the construction of the moduli space of polarized abelian
varieties of a given type. In the first part the Siegel upper half space ‚Ñåùëîis introduced.
It is shown that it parametrizes all polarized abelian varieties of a given type ùê∑with
a symplectic basis (Theorem 3.1.2). Then the group ùê∫ùê∑is introduced and its action
on ‚Ñåùëîis worked out. Finally, the moduli space of polarized abelian varieties of type
ùê∑is constructed as a normal complex analytic space (Theorem 3.1.12).

show that it parametrizes the set of polarized abelian varieties of this type with a
symplectic basis. Moreover, we work out what it means for two points of ‚Ñåùëîthat the
associated polarized abelian varieties are isomorphic.

Our main aim is the construction of the moduli space of polarized abelian varieties

Remark 3.1.10 (1): Consider the group ùêæ:= Sp2ùëî(R)‚à©O2ùëî(R) of Proposition 3.1.7.

(2): Given ùê∑, one can always find ùê∑‚Ä≤ such that ùê∫ùê∑‚Ä≤ ‚äÜùê∫ùê∑, of finite index, which

of it.

3.1.4 The Moduli Space of Polarized Abelian Varieties of Type ùë´
Let ùê∫be a group acting as a group of isomorphisms on a complex manifold ùëã. The

space in a natural way: denote by ùúã: ùëã‚Üíùëã/ùê∫the canonical projection. Then by
definition, for ùëà‚äÜùëã/ùê∫open, Oùëã/ùê∫(ùëà) is the set of functions ùëì: ùëà‚ÜíC for which
ùëìùúãis holomorphic on ùúã‚àí1(ùëà). We will use without proof the following theorem due
to Cartan (see [30], see also Theorem 2.3.17).

manifold ùëã. Then the quotient ùëã/ùê∫is a normal complex analytic space.

3.2 Level Structures
Given a type ùê∑, we saw in Theorem 3.1.2 that the Siegel upper half space ‚Ñåùëîcan
be considered as the moduli space of polarized abelian varieties of type ùê∑with a
symplectic basis. A symplectic basis cannot be defined in algebraic terms. A level
structure on a polarized abelian variety is roughly speaking an algebraic replacement
of the notion of a symplectic basis or only some properties of it. The corresponding
moduli spaces are quotients of ‚Ñåùëîby suitable subgroups of ùê∫ùê∑(respectively Œìùê∑)
and hence are situated between ‚Ñåùëîand Aùê∑(respectively e
Aùê∑).
Moduli spaces of polarized abelian varieties with level structures have various
applications in arithmetic and algebraic geometry. In this section we present the most
important examples. For other examples, see Section 3.5.1 and Exercises 3.2.3 (3)
to (6).

3.2.2 Generalized Level ùíè-structures
A level ùëõ-structure on a principally polarized abelian variety (ùëã, ùêª) is by definition
a level (ùëõ1ùëî)-structure on the polarized abelian variety (ùëã, ùëõùêª) in the sense of the
previous section. So a level ùê∑-structure is a generalization of this notion. In this
section a different generalization is given.
Let (ùëã= ùëâ/Œõ, ùêª) be a polarized abelian variety of (arbitrary) type ùê∑and ùëõ
a positive integer. A symplectic basis ùúÜ1, . . . , ùúÜùëî, ùúá1, . . . ùúáùëîof Œõ for ùêªdetermines
a basis for the ùëõ-division points ùëãùëõin ùëã, namely 1
ùëõùúÜ1, . . . , 1
ùëõùúÜùëî, 1
ùëõùúá1, . . . , 1
ùëõùúáùëî. A
generalized level ùëõ-structure for (ùëã, ùêª) is by definition a basis of ùëãùëõcoming from
a symplectic basis in this way.
For any ùëõ> 1 the principal congruence subgroup Œìùê∑(ùëõ) of Œìùê∑is defined to be
Œìùê∑(ùëõ) := {ùëÖ‚ààŒìùê∑| ùëÖ‚â°12ùëîmod ùëõ}.
Note that Œìùê∑(ùëõ) is a normal subgroup of Œìùê∑.

The proof is completely analogous to the proof of Theorem 3.2.1. So we leave it

3.3 The Theta Transformation Formula
Given a type ùê∑, theta functions on Cùëîwith respect to a lattice Œõùëç= (ùëç, ùê∑)Z2ùëî(for
ùëç‚àà‚Ñåùëî) are holomorphic functions on Cùëîwith a certain functional behaviour with
respect to translations with elements of Œõùëç. Varying ùëçwithin ‚Ñåùëîby the action of
the symplectic group on ‚Ñåùëî, one may ask how the corresponding theta functions are
related. The answer is given by the classical Theta Transformation Formula.

3.3.2 Classical Theta Functions
In Section 1.5.2 we introduced classical theta functions. For the proof of the final
version of the theta transformation formula we need some more of their properties.
For example, that they depend holomorphically on ùëç‚àà‚Ñåùëî. We start by introducing
the relation between the canonical theta functions ùúóùëê
ùëçand the classical Riemann

3.4 The Universal Family
Given a type ùê∑, a family ùëù: ùîõùê∑‚Üí‚Ñåùëîis constructed which parametrizes all abelian
varieties ùëãùëçwith ùëç‚àà‚Ñåùëî, polarization of type ùê∑and symplectic basis. Recall that
a family of abelian varieties (of type ùê∑and with symplectic basis) is a holomorphic
map of analytic varieties ùëû: X ‚Üíùëásuch that every fibre ùëû‚àí1(ùë°) is an abelian variety
(with polarization of type ùê∑and symplectic basis depending holomorphically on ùë°).
The family ùëù: ùîõùê∑‚Üí‚Ñåùëîis universal in the following sense: Given any family ùëûas
above, there is a unique holomorphic map ùëì: ùëá‚Üí‚Ñåùëîsuch that ùëì‚àó(ùëù) = ùëû. We will
not prove the universality of the family ùëù, but see Exercise 3.4.5 (7).

In Theorem 3.1.11 Cartan‚Äôs theorem was recalled, which says that the quotient of
a complex manifold by a group acting properly and discontinuously is a normal
complex analytic space. Clearly the singularities of the quotient correspond to points
with non-trivial stabilizer. Hence if the group in addition acts freely, the quotient is
a manifold. So we immediately obtain from this and the definitions the first part of
the following proposition.
Proposition 3.4.1 The quotient

3 Moduli Spaces
3.5 Projective Embeddings of Moduli Spaces
We saw in Section 3.4.3 that the map ùúëùê∑: ùîõùê∑‚ÜíPùëÅgiven by a certain sublinear
system of the line bundle ùîèon ùîõùê∑is holomorphic if only ùëë1 ‚â•2. If ùê∫is any
subgroup of finite index in ùê∫ùê∑(ùê∑), we saw in Proposition 3.4.8 that the quotient
ùîõùê∑/ùê∫admits the structure of a family of abelian varieties, if ùëë1 ‚â•3. The main
result of this section is that, if ùê∫is the group of orthogonal level ùê∑-structures and
ùëë1 ‚â•4, then the map ùúëùê∑: ùîõùê∑‚ÜíPùëõgoes down to a holomorphic embedding of
the corresponding moduli space of abelian varieties.

Corollary 3.5.7 The space Aùê∑(ùê∑)0 is a quasi-projective variety of dimension
1
2ùëî(ùëî+ 1).
Proof Denote for a moment by ùëãthe image of the analytic embedding
ùúìùê∑: Aùê∑(ùê∑)0 ‚Ü©‚ÜíPùëÅof Theorem 3.5.4 and let ùëãbe its closure in PùëÅwith
respect to the euclidean topology. Using the theory of modular forms, it is not dif-
ficult to show that ùëãcoincides with the closure of ùëãwith respect to the Zariski
topology (see Igusa [70, Theorem V.8]). So ùëãis a projective algebraic variety by
Chow‚Äôs Theorem 2.1.16. Moreover, by Igusa [70, Remark page 224], the variety ùëãis
Zariski open in ùëã. This implies the assertion, since dim ùëã= dim ‚Ñåùëî= 1
2ùëî(ùëî+ 1).‚ñ°
Corollary 3.5.8 Let ùê∫be a subgroup of finite index in ùê∫ùê∑. Then Aùê∫:= ‚Ñåùëî/ùê∫is
an algebraic variety. In particular, most of the moduli space occurring above are
algebraic.
Proof Since ùê∫ùê∑(ùê∑)0 is of finite index in ùê∫ùê∑, so is the intersection of all its
conjugates, say e
Gùê∑(ùê∑)0 in ùê∫ùê∑. Let
e
Aùê∑(ùê∑)0 = ‚Ñåùëî/ e
Gùê∑(ùê∑)0.
Then the natural map e
Aùê∑(ùê∑)0 ‚ÜíAùê∑(ùê∑)0 is finite. Since Aùê∑(ùê∑)0 is algebraic
by Corollary 3.5.7 and a finite cover of an algebraic variety, ramified at most in an
algebraic set, is algebraic, e
Aùê∑(ùê∑)0 is algebraic.
Now e
Aùê∑(ùê∑)0 being normal in ùê∫ùê∑, the quotient ùê∫ùê∑/ e
Aùê∑(ùê∑)0 is a finite group,
acting on e
Aùê∑(ùê∑)0 with quotient Aùê∑. Since the quotient of an algebraic variety by
a finite group is algebraic, this gives that Aùê∑is algebraic.
Finally, as a finite covering of the algebraic variety Aùê∑with at most algebraic
ramification, the variety Aùê∫is algebraic.
‚ñ°
Remark 3.5.9 The moduli space Aùëî:= A1ùëîof principally polarized abelian vari-
eties of dimension ùëîis not compact. There are several toroidal compactifications of
Aùëî, the most prominent being the second Voronoi compactification Aùëî. For these
compactifications (as well as for the other moduli spaces) consider the book of Ash,
Mumford, Rapoport and Tai [10].

Chapter 4
Jacobian Varieties
A curve in this chapter means a complex smooth projective curve. To every curve ùê∂
one can associate in a natural way a principally polarized abelian variety, its Jacobian
ùêΩ(ùê∂). As we mentioned already in the introduction, the theory of abelian varieties
originated with the investigation of Jacobians. They are not only the most important,
but also the best-known examples of abelian varieties. Much more can be said about
them than about a general principally polarized abelian variety. In fact, presenting
the theory of Jacobian varieties in a satisfactory way would require a whole volume
for itself. This chapter contains, apart from the basic definitions and constructions,
only some selected topics on Jacobian varieties.
Section 4.1 contains the basic definitions of the Jacobian ùêΩ(ùê∂) of a curve ùê∂.
Moreover, it is shown that the Abel‚ÄìJacobi map ùê∂‚ÜíùêΩ(ùê∂) is an embedding.
Without proof we state the Abel‚ÄìJacobi Theorem, the proof of which is contained
in almost every book on compact Riemann surfaces or algebraic curves. In the
second section the theta divisor of a Jacobian variety is studied. We prove Poincar√©‚Äôs
formula and Riemann‚Äôs theorem and state without proof Riemann‚Äôs Singularity
Theorem. As a consequence we can compute the number of even and odd theta
characteristics, as well as the dimension of the singularity locus of the theta divisor.
Section 4.3 contains a proof of the Torelli Theorem. In Sections 4.4 and 4.5 we
construct the Poincar√© bundles of a curve ùê∂out of the Poincar√© bundle for ùêΩ(ùê∂)
(constructed in Section 1.4.4) and derive the universal property of the Jacobian. In
the sixth section it is shown that the endomorphism ring of the Jacobian ùêΩ(ùê∂) may
be interpreted as the ring of equivalence classes of correspondences on ùê∂, a result
which is probably due to Hurwitz [68]. Proposition 4.6.12, expressing the rational
trace of an endomorphism of ùêΩ(ùê∂) in terms of an associated correspondence of
ùê∂, is due to Weil [137]. In Section 4.7 we prove an improvement of a criterion of
Matsusaka [91] for a principally polarized abelian variety (in terms of a 1-cycle) to
be the Jacobian of a curve, the improvement due to Ran [109]. The proof given here
is due to Collino [32].
201
¬© The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2023 
H. Lange, Abelian Varieties over the Complex Numbers, Grundlehren Text Editions, 
https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-25570-0_4

202
4 Jacobian Varieties
It is in general difficult to compute the period matrix of the Jacobian of a curve
which is given in terms of equations. Under the supervision of F. Klein, Bolza
developed in his thesis [26] a method for doing this, provided that the curve admits
a sufficiently large group of automorphisms. We explain the method and give an
example in Section 4.8.
We use some basic results of the theory of algebraic curves, such as the Riemann‚Äì
Roch Theorem, the Pl√ºcker Formulas, and the description of finite coverings. By
ùëîùëü
ùëëwe denote a linear system of dimension ùëüand degree ùëëon a curve ùê∂. Finally,
we use Bertini‚Äôs Theorem and the inequality for the dimension of the non-empty
intersection of two closed subvarieties of a smooth projective variety.
4.1 Definitions and Basic Results
This section contains the definition of the Jacobian ùêΩ(ùê∂) of a curve ùê∂as well as
the canonical principal polarization of ùêΩ(ùê∂). Moreover, the Abel‚ÄìJacobi map is
investigated.

4.1.3 The Abel‚ÄìJacobi Map
There is another approach to defining the Jacobian variety of ùê∂, namely as the group
Pic0(ùê∂) of line bundles of degree zero on ùê∂using the Abel‚ÄìJacobi Theorem, which
will be explained now.

Jacobi‚Äôs Inversion Theorem states that the Abel‚ÄìJacobi map is surjective (see
Griffiths‚ÄìHarris [55, p. 235]). On the other hand, by a theorem of Abel its kernel
is the subgroup of principal divisors in Div0(ùê∂) (see Griffith-Harris [55, p. 235]).
Combining both theorems we obtain the following result.
Theorem 4.1.4 (Abel‚ÄìJacobi Theorem) The Abel‚ÄìJacobi map induces a canonical
isomorphism
Pic0(ùê∂)
‚àº
‚àí‚ÜíùêΩ(ùê∂).
Via this isomorphism Pic0(ùê∂) inherits the structure of a principally polarized
abelian variety. For the proof of the Abel‚ÄìJacobi Theorem we refer to the standard
books on Riemann surfaces or algebraic curves. In the sequel we identify
ùêΩ(ùê∂) = Pic0(ùê∂)
via the canonical isomorphism. We work with both interpretations without further
notice: sometimes we consider elements of ùêΩ(ùê∂) as points and write + for the group
law and sometimes as line bundles on ùê∂and write ‚äófor the group law. The respective
meaning will be clear from the context.

define more generally an Abel‚ÄìJacobi map from Divùëõ(ùê∂) to ùêΩ(ùê∂). Certainly, for

4.2 The Theta Divisor
Let (ùêΩ, Œò) be the Jacobian of a smooth projective curve ùê∂of genus ùëî‚â•1. In this
section we study the geometry of the theta divisors Œò. In particular we will see that
some properties of Œò reflect geometrical properties of the curve itself.
4.2.1 Poincar√©‚Äôs Formula

classes of the subset of Picùëõ(ùê∂) of line bundles with non-empty linear system and
an intersection power of Œò.

4.2.2 Riemann‚Äôs Theorem
There is no canonical way to distinguish a theta divisor Œò in ùêΩ(ùê∂) within its algebraic
equivalence class. On the other hand, the divisor ùëäùëî‚àí1 in Picùëî‚àí1(ùê∂) is intrinsic. So
the line bundle ùúÇin Corollary 4.2.4 depends on the choice of Œò. If Œò is one of the
22ùëîsymmetric theta divisors (see Lemma 2.3.5), then we can say more about the
line bundle ùúÇ. For this recall that a theta characteristic on ùê∂is a line bundle ùúÖon ùê∂
with ùúÖ2 = ùúîùê∂.
Theorem 4.2.5 (Riemann‚Äôs Theorem) For any symmetric theta divisor Œò there is
a theta characteristic ùúÖon ùê∂such that
ùëäùëî‚àí1 = ùõº‚àó
ùúÖŒò.
In classical terminology Riemann‚Äôs Theorem reads:
ùëäùëî‚àí1 ‚àíùúÖ= Œò.
In view of Riemann‚Äôs Theorem, it makes sense to call ùëäùëî‚àí1 the canonical theta
divisor of ùê∂. Note that in particular the theta divisor Œò corresponding to a symplectic
basis is symmetric. To be more precise, let ùúÜ1, . . . , ùúÜ2ùëîbe a symplectic basis for the

canonical polarization and ùêø0 the corresponding line bundle of characteristic zero

case the theta characteristic ùúÖin the theorem is called Riemann‚Äôs constant.

On the other hand, together with the existence theorem of Brill‚ÄìNoether theory (see
Arbarello et al [8, p. 206]), the proof gives that sing ùëäùëî‚àí1 is equidimensional for
non-hyperelliptic ùê∂.

4.3 The Torelli Theorem
217
4.3 The Torelli Theorem
There are many proofs of the Torelli Theorem, for example given in Torelli [133]
(1913), Comessatti [34] (1914‚Äì15), Weil [139] (1957), Andreotti [5] (1958), Mat-
susaka [90] (1958), Martens [88] (1963) and Beilinson‚ÄìPolishuk [19] (2001). Al-
though proofs can be found in almost every book on compact Riemann surfaces or
algebraic curves, I think a proof should be contained in an introductory book on
abelian varieties.
The proof given here is due to Andreotti [5] with its improvement in Arbarello
et al [8], which is probably the easiest proof. Moreover it fits best into this volume,
because it uses the Gauss map which we used already for other purposes.
4.3.1 Statement of the Theorem
To every smooth projective curve ùê∂we associated its principally polarized Jacobian
(ùêΩ(ùê∂), Œò). The Torelli Theorem says that conversely a principally polarized Jacobian
(ùêΩ, Œò) determines the curve. In other words:
Theorem 4.3.1 (Torelli Theorem) The map from the set of isomorphism classes of
smooth projective curves of genus ùëîto the moduli space of principally polarized
abelian varieties of dimension ùëî
ùê∂‚Ü¶‚Üí(ùêΩ(ùê∂), Œò)
is injective.
The proof uses the Gauss map, which was already introduced in Section 2.1.2.
However, here we need a little more.
4.3.2 The Gauss Map of a Canonically Polarized Jacobian
Let ùê∂be a smooth curve of genus ùëî‚â•2 with Jacobian (ùêΩ, Œò). As above let Œòùë†
denote the smooth part of Œò. According to Section 2.1.2 and Exercise 2.1.6 (6), the
Gauss map

is defined by associating to every point ùë•‚ààŒòùë†the translate to the origin of the pro-
jectivized tangent hyperplane ùëÉ(ùëáùë•Œò). Since ùê∫does not change under a translation,
we assume that ùêΩ= Picùëî‚àí1(ùê∂) and Œò = ùëäùëî‚àí1, the theta-divisor of Lemma 4.2.1, that
is, the image under the canonical map
ùúå: ùê∂(ùëî‚àí1) ‚ÜíPicùëî‚àí1(ùê∂).

4.3 The Torelli Theorem
221
4.3.3 Proof of the Torelli Theorem
Given the principally polarized abelian variety (ùêΩ(ùê∂), Œò), we have to show that one
can reconstruct the curve ùê∂from it. But it is a classical theorem that the double dual
of any projective variety is the variety itself:
(ùëã‚àó)‚àó= ùëã
(see Harris [60, Theorem 15.24]). Using this, in the non-hyperelliptic case, the Torelli
Theorem follows from Proposition 4.3.4. In the hyperelliptic case it follows from
Proposition 4.3.5, since every ùëù‚àó
ùëñdetermines of course ùëùùëñ, which is a branch point of
the double cover on ùê∂0. This gives the hyperelliptic double cover ùê∂of ùê∂0 and thus
completes the proof.

(5) (Torelli Problem for Complex Tori of Dimension 2) Let ùëãbe a complex torus
of dimension 2 and ùúîa non-vanishing holomorphic 2-form on ùëã. The map
ùúãùëã: ùêª2(ùëã, Z) ‚ÜíC, ùõæ‚Ü¶‚Üí
‚à´
ùõæùúîis called the period of ùëã(with respect to 2-forms).
Since ùúîis unique up to a nonzero constant, so is the period ùúãùëã. Given another
complex torus ùëåof dimension 2 with period ùúãùëå, we say that an isomorphism
ùúë: ùêª2(ùëã, Z) ‚Üíùêª2(ùëå, Z) preserves the periods if ùúãùëãùúë= ùëêùúãùëåfor some non-zero
constant ùëê. Assume that there exists an isomorphism ùúë: ùêª2(ùëã, Z) ‚Üíùêª2(ùëå, Z)
preserving the intersection form and the periods. Show that either ùëå‚âÉùëãor
ùëå‚âÉbùëã.
In particular, for self-dual complex tori of dimension 2 the intersection form
and the period determine the complex torus. This is called Torelli‚Äôs Theorem for
self-dual 2-dimensional complex tori.
(See Shioda [125].)
4.4 The Poincar√© Bundles for a Curve ùë™
4.4.1 Definition of the Bundle Pùíè

4.5 The Universal Property of the Jacobian
The Jacobian ùêΩof a curve ùê∂of genus ùëîadmits a universal property: maps from ùê∂
into abelian varieties factorize via the Jacobian. In this section a proof and some
applications will be given.

4.6 Endomorphisms Associated to Curves and Divisors
In this section we prove the criterion of Matsusaka [91] for numerical equivalence
of 1-cycles, respectively algebraic equivalence of divisors, in terms of the endomor-
phisms associated to cycles. The criterion is valid for an arbitrary abelian variety,
but the proof uses the theory of Jacobians and in particular some results on corre-
spondences.
4.6.1 Correspondences Between Curves
The main result here is that every homomorphism between Jacobians can be de-
scribed by correspondences. We associate to every such homomorphism ùõæa corre-
spondence ùêøùõæwhich can be used to compute the rational trace of ùõæ.

4.6.2 Endomorphisms Associated to Cycles
In this section let ùëãmore generally be an abelian variety of dimension ùëîand denote by
ùëâand ùëäalgebraic cycles on ùëãof complementary dimension. There is a canonical
way to associate to the pair (ùëâ, ùëä) an endomorphism ùõø(ùëâ, ùëä) of ùëã. Following
Morikawa [94] and Matsusaka [91], we will show that ùõø(ùëâ, ùëä) depends only on the
algebraic equivalence classes of ùëâand ùëä.
Here we consider only algebraic cycles ùëâwith coefficients in Z, that is, finite
formal sums

4 Jacobian Varieties
4.7 The Criterion of Matsusaka‚ÄìRan
This section contains a proof of the criterion of Matsusaka‚ÄìRan (see Ran [109]) for
a polarized abelian variety to be a product of Jacobians. We give here a modified
version of Collino‚Äôs proof (see Collino [32]).
4.7.1 Statement of the Criterion
Recall that a curve ùê∂on an abelian variety ùëãis said to generate ùëãif ùëãis the smallest
abelian variety containing ùê∂. More generally, an effective algebraic 1-cycle √ç ùëüùúàùê∂ùúà
on ùëã, ùëüùúà> 0 for all ùúà, generates ùëãif the union of the curves ùê∂ùúàgenerates ùëã.
Theorem 4.7.1 (Criterion of Matsusaka‚ÄìRan) Suppose (ùëã, ùêø) is a polarized
abelian variety of dimension ùëîand ùê∂= √çùëõ
ùúà=1 ùëüùúàùê∂ùúàis an effective 1-cycle gen-
erating ùëãwith (ùê∂¬∑ ùêø) = ùëî. Then ùëü1 = ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ = ùëüùëõ= 1, the curves ùê∂ùúàare smooth, and
(ùëã, ùêø) is isomorphic to the product of the canonically polarized Jacobians of the
ùê∂ùúà‚Äôs:
(ùëã, ùêø) ‚âÉ(ùêΩ(ùê∂1), Œò1) √ó ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ √ó (ùêΩ(ùê∂ùëõ), Œòùëõ).
In particular, if ùê∂is an irreducible curve generating ùëãwith (ùê∂¬∑ ùêø) = ùëî, then ùê∂
is smooth and (ùëã, ùêø) is the Jacobian of ùê∂. As a special case we get Matsusaka‚Äôs
criterion.
Corollary 4.7.2 (Matsusaka‚Äôs Criterion) Let (ùëã, Œòùëã) be a principally polarized
abelian variety of dimension ùëîand ùê∂‚äÇùëãan irreducible curve with
[ùê∂] =

‚ñ°
Corollary 4.7.3
(a) A principally polarized abelian surface is either the Jacobian of a smooth curve
of genus 2 or the canonically polarized product of two elliptic curves.
(b) A principally polarized abelian threefold is either the Jacobian of a smooth curve
of genus 3 or the principally polarized product of an abelian surface with an
elliptic curve respectively three elliptic curves.

4.7 The Criterion of Matsusaka‚ÄìRan
241
Proof a) is an immediate consequence of the criterion and Riemann‚ÄìRoch.
(b): The moduli spaces of curves of genus 3 and of principally polarized abelian
threefolds are both irreducible algebraic varieties of dimension 6. Hence by the Torelli
Theorem 4.3.1 a general principally polarized abelian threefold is a Jacobian. Since
under specialization effective 1-cycles go to effective 1-cycles and the intersection
numbers are preserved, Theorem 4.7.1 implies the assertion.
‚ñ°
The argument of the last sentence of the proof of Corollary 4.7.3 yields more
generally:
Corollary 4.7.4 Any specialization of a Jacobian is a product of Jacobians.
