‚ñ°
Remark 4.7.5 (The Schottky Problem) Matsusaka‚Äôs theorem, Corollary 4.7.2, is
a good criterion for a principally polarized abelian variety ùëãto be the Jacobian of
a curve. However it assumes the existence of suitable curve in ùëã. One would like
a criterion without a curve. Moreover, one would like information about where the
Jacobians of genus ùëîlie in the moduli space A1g of principally polarized abelian
varieties of dimension ùëî.
We never spoke about the moduli space of curves of a fixed genus ùëî. Taking for
granted that it exists and is an irreducible algebraic variety Mùëîof dimension 3ùëî‚àí3
(see Mumford et al [101], in this remark we assume ùëî‚â•2), we conclude from
Torelli‚Äôs Theorem 4.3.1 that the map
Mùëî‚ÜíA1g,
ùê∂‚Ü¶‚ÜíùêΩ(ùê∂)
is injective and hence its image is an irreducible subvariety of A1g of the same
dimension as Mùëî. According to Corollary 4.7.4 its closure in A1g, which we denote
by Mùëî, is a closed algebraic subvariety of dimension 3ùëî‚àí3. The Schottky problem
consists in describing Mùëîby equations in A1g or more generally to give criteria for
a Jacobian.
There are a lot of papers concerning this problem of which I mention only a few.
First of all, since dim Mùëî= 3ùëî‚àí3 and dim A1g = 1
2ùëî(ùëî+ 1) we get Mùëî= A1g for
ùëî= 2 and 3. So we may assume ùëî‚â•4.
In 1888 Schottky showed in [120] that a certain polynomial of degree 16 in the
theta constants vanishes on M4, but not everywhere on A14. Igusa showed in [71]
that the zero set of this polynomial equals M4.

4.7 The Criterion of Matsusaka‚ÄìRan
245
In 1909 Schottky and Jung constructed in [121] expressions in the theta constants
which vanish on Mùëîby means of what is now called the theory of Prym varieties
(see Section 5.3 below). For the Schottky‚ÄìJung relations see [24, 12.10.6]. These
expressions define a certain locus Sùëîin A1g, called the Schottky locus, which contains
Mùëî. It is conjectured that Sùëî= Mùëî. Van Geemen showed in [48] that Mùëîis an
irreducible component of Sùëî.
There are several geometric approaches to the Schottky problem. Let us mention
only one. Consider the abelian varieties ùëãùëç= Cùëî/Œõùëçassociated to ùëç‚àà‚Ñåùëîwith
theta divisor Œò. According to Theorem 2.3.20 the map ùúë2Œò induces an embedding
of the corresponding Kummer variety ùêæùëçinto P2ùëî‚àí1. If now ùëãùëçis the Jacobian of
a curve, then ùêæùëçadmits a trisecant in P2ùëî‚àí1. This is the content of Fay‚Äôs trisecant
identity not proved in this volume (but see [24, 11.10.1]). It was conjectured that if
the Kummer variety of ùëãùëç‚ààAùëîadmits a trisecant, then ùëãùëçis a Jacobian. Beauville
and Debarre proved in [18] that the existence of a trisecant implies dim sing Œò ‚â•4
which is a hint that the conjecture might be true. Finally Krichever proved this
conjecture in [79]. To be more precise, he proved that if the Kummer variety of an
indecomposable ùëãùëç‚ààAùëîadmits a trisecant (which may be degenerate; that is, a
trisecant with multiplicities), such that no point of intersection of this line with the
Kummer is singular in ùêæùëç, then ùëãùëçis the Jacobian of a curve.
An analytic approach to the Schottky problem is given as follows: there is an
infinitesimal version of the trisecant identity, where the three points of ùêæùëçare
replaced by three tangent vectors of ùëãùëçat 0 or equivalently by three constant vector
fields on ùëãùëç. Consider the theta functions ùúÉ

This equation is called the KP-equation (after Kadomtsev‚ÄìPetviashvili). The equation
characterizes Jacobians in A1g. This was conjectured by Novikov and proved by
Shiota in [128].
A completely algebraic-geometric proof of both theorems,ofShiotaandKrichever,
was given by Arbarello, Codogni and Pareschi in [9].
So in a sense both results give a proof of the Schottky problem. But there remain
many other interesting approaches to the problem which are open or only partly
solved. A good survey is given by Grushevsky in [59].

4.8 A Method to Compute the Period Matrix of a Jacobian
Given a smooth projective curve ùê∂of genus ùëî, it is difficult in general to compute a
period matrix for its Jacobian ùêΩ(ùê∂) = ùêª0(ùúîùê∂)‚àó/ùêª1(ùê∂, Z). However, if ùê∂admits a
sufficiently large group of automorphisms, there is a method, due to Bolza [26], for
doing this. We want to explain it, work out an example, and state Bolza‚Äôs result as
well as several other examples in the exercises in Section 4.8.3.

Chapter 5
Main Examples of Abelian Varieties
In this chapter some of the most prominent examples of abelian varieties will be
introduced, namely abelian surfaces, Picard and Albanese varieties, Prym varieties
and Intermediate Jacobians.
Section 5.1 deals with abelian surfaces; that is, abelian varieties ùëãof dimension
2. A polarization ùêø‚ààPic(ùëã) is of type (ùëë1, ùëë2) for positive integers ùëë1, ùëë2 with ùëë1
dividing ùëë2. For ùëë1 ‚â•3 the map ùúëùêø: ùëã‚ÜíùëÉ(ùêª0(ùêø)‚àó) is an embedding according
Lefschetz Theorem 2.1.10. If ùëë1 = 2, the behaviour of ùúëùêøis worked out explicitly in
Sections 2.2.4 and 2.3.6. It remains to study the behaviour of ùúëùêøin the case ùëë1 = 1.
The main result of this section is a proof of a criterion for ùúëùêøto be an embedding,
due to Reider [111], Theorem 5.1.6 below.
To every smooth projective curve ùê∂one can associate an abelian variety, its
Jacobian ùêΩ(ùê∂). The same construction generalizes to give the Picard and Albanese
varieties Pic0(ùëÄ) and Alb(ùëÄ) for every smooth projective variety ùëÄ. They are
introduced in Section 5.2 and it is shown that they are dual to each other. In the
special case of a curve ùê∂this gives the self-duality of ùêΩ(ùê∂).
In Section 5.3 we introduce and study the Prym variety of a finite covering
ùëì: ùê∂‚Üíùê∂‚Ä≤ of smooth projective curves. The pullback of line bundle induces
an homomorphism ùëì‚àó: ùêΩ(ùê∂‚Ä≤) ‚ÜíùêΩ(ùê∂), which is an isogeny onto its image ùê¥‚äÇ
ùêΩ(ùê∂). The complement ùëÉ= ùëÉ( ùëì) of ùê¥in ùêΩ(ùê∂) with respect to the canonical
polarization of ùêΩ(ùê∂) is called the Prym variety of ùëìif the restriction of the canonical
polarization Œò of ùêΩ(ùê∂) to ùëÉis a multiple of a principal polarization Œû on ùëÉ. Theorem
5.3.9 determines all coverings which lead to Prym varieties. Section 5.3.3 gives a
topological construction of Prym varieties.
For a smooth projective variety ùëÄof dimension ùëõ‚â•3 there are several possi-
bilities to associate an abelian variety. The Picard and Albanese varieties use the
first cohomology and the (2ùëõ‚àí1)-th cohomology of ùëÄfor this. One can also use
the intermediate cohomologies in order to associate abelian varieties to ùëÄ. However
there are several ways to do this, which lead to different intermediate Jacobians.
In Section 5.4.2 we introduce the Griffiths Intermediate Jacobian, which is a non-

5.1 Abelian Surfaces
An abelian surface is by definition an abelian variety of dimension 2. A polarization
ùêøon an abelian surface ùëãis of type (ùëë1, ùëë2). If ùëë1 ‚â•3, the associated map
ùúëùêø: ùëã‚ÜíPùëÅis an embedding according to the Theorem of Lefschetz 2.1.10.
Theorems 2.2.1, 2.2.8 and 2.3.21 work out the behaviour of ùúëùêøin the case ùëë1 = 2.
In this section we prove a criterion, due to Reider, for ùúëùêøto be an embedding in the
case ùëë1 = 1.

5.1.2 Rank-2 Bundles on an Abelian Surface
In the next section we need some properties of rank-2 vector bundles on a polarized

A vector bundle ùêπof rank 2 on ùëãis called ùúá-semistable (with respect to a

(ùëê1(ùê∏) ¬∑ ùêø) ‚â§1

always strict.

with a torsion-free quotient sheaf. On the other hand, any coherent subsheaf of

ùúá-(semi-)stability it suffices to require the inequality (5.3) for all line bundles con-

5.1.3 Reider‚Äôs Theorem
Suppose ùêøis an ample line bundle of type (1, ùëë) with ùëë‚â•5 on an abelian surface ùëã.
The object of this section is to give a criterion for the induced map ùúëùêø: ùëã‚ÜíPùëë‚àí1 to
be an embedding. In order to avoid trivialities, suppose (ùëã, ùêø) does not split; that is,
is not isomorphic to a polarized product of elliptic curves (ùê∏1 √ó ùê∏2, ùëù‚àó
1ùêø1 ‚äóùëù‚àó
2ùêø2).
The main result of this section is
Theorem 5.1.6 (Reider‚Äôs Theorem) Suppose ùêøis an ample line bundle of type
(1, ùëë) with ùëë‚â•5 on ùëã. Then the morphism ùúëùêø: ùëã‚ÜíPùëë‚àí1 is an embedding if and
only if there is no elliptic curve ùê∂on ùëãwith (ùê∂¬∑ ùêø) = 2.
A general abelian surface ùëãwith polarization ùêøof type (1, ùëë) does not contain an
elliptic curve, since its N√©ron Severi group NS(ùëã) is isomorphic to Z (see Exercise
3.1.5 (6)). So generically the map ùúëùêøis an embedding.
On the other hand, special abelian surfaces ùëãmay contain infinitely many elliptic
curves. For applications, in particular to moduli problems, it is more useful to have
a criterion involving only one curve: according to Exercise 1.5.5 (6) there is a cyclic
isogeny of abelian varieties ùëû: ùëã‚Üíùëåof degree ùëëand a line bundle ùëÄon ùëåwith
ùêø= ùëû‚àóùëÄ. The line bundle ùëÄdefines a principal polarization on ùëå. According to
Corollary 4.7.3 the unique curve ùê∑ùëåin |ùëÄ| is either smooth of genus 2 or a union
of two elliptic curves intersecting exactly in one point. Define ùê∑ùëãby the cartesian
diagram

5.1.4 Proof of the Theorem
It suffices to show that ùúëùêøis an embedding provided that there is no elliptic curve ùê∂
in ùëãwith (ùê∂¬∑ ùêø) = 2, the converse implication being trivial, since a line bundle of
degree 2 on an elliptic curve is never very ample.

5.1.5 Exercises and Further Results
(1) Use Theorem 2.6.2 to see that the endomorphism algebra of an elliptic curve
is either Q or an imaginary quadratic field.
(2) Use Theorem 2.6.2 and the previous exercise to see that only the following
endomorphism algebras EndQ(ùëã) are possible for an abelian surface ùëã:
(a) A non-simple ùëãis isogenous to the product of elliptic curves ùëã‚àºùê∏1 √ó ùê∏2.
(i) If ùê∏1 and ùê∏2 are non-isogenous, then EndQ(ùëã) ‚âÉEndQ(ùê∏1) ‚äï
EndQ(ùê∏2).
(ii) If ùê∏1 and ùê∏2 are isogenous, then EndQ(ùëã) ‚âÉM2(EndùëÑ(ùê∏1)).
(b) If ùëãis simple, then
(i) EndùëÑ(ùëã) ‚âÉQ or a real quadratic field;
(ii) EndùëÑ(ùëã) is either a totally definite of a totally indefinite quaternion
algebra over Q;
(iii) EndùëÑ(ùëã) is a totally complex quadratic extension of either Q or a real
quadratic field.
(3) Let ùëãbe a complex torus of dimension 2 with period matrix (ùëñùëå, 12), where
ùëå= (ùë¶ùëñùëó) ‚ààM2(R).

ùúå(ùë•) = 4 ‚àídimQ(ùë¶11, ùë¶12, ùë¶21, ùë¶22) +

realizing all possible values for the Picard number ùúå= ùúå(ùëã) and the
algebraic dimension ùëé= ùëé(ùëã) (defined in Chapter 2). Here ùëù, ùëû, ùëüdenote

5.2 Albanese and Picard Varieties
In this section we generalize the notion of a Jacobian of a smooth projective curve
to higher-dimensional varieties. More generally we associate to any compact K√§hler
manifold ùëÄof dimension ùëõ‚â•1 two complex tori, the Albanese torus Alb(ùëÄ)
and the Picard torus Pic0(ùëÄ). If ùëÄis a smooth projective variety, they are abelian
varieties and in fact dual to each other.

5.3 Prym Varieties

5.3 Prym Varieties
Given a finite covering ùëì: ùê∂‚Üíùê∂‚Ä≤ with Jacobians ùêΩand ùêΩ‚Ä≤, the complement ùëÉ
of ùëì‚àóùêΩ‚Ä≤ in ùêΩwith respect to the canonical polarization is called a Prym variety if
the restriction of the canonical polarization of ùêΩto ùëÉis a multiple of a principal
polarization. We determine all coverings ùëìleading to a Prym variety and give a
topological construction of the most important ones. For more on Prym varieties,
see Chapter 2 of [83].
5.3.1 Abelian Subvarieties of a Principally Polarized Abelian Variety
In Section 2.4.3 we introduced the notion of complementary abelian subvarieties of
a polarized abelian variety (ùëã, ùêø) and studied some first properties. In this section
we derive further results on such subvarieties in the special case of a principal
polarization ùêø= Oùëã(Œò).
Let (ùëã, Œò) be a principally polarized abelian variety and ùúÑ= ùúÑùëå: ùëå‚Ü©‚Üíùëãan

for the isogeny of the induced polarization. Recall that the exponent ùëí(ùëå) of ùëåis
defined as the exponent of the finite group Ker ùúôùëå. According to Proposition 1.1.15
the map ùúìùëå= ùëí(ùëå)ùúô‚àí1
ùëåis an isogeny. With this notation the norm-endomorphism

ùúÄùëå= ùúÑùúô‚àí1

Corollary 5.3.2 Complementary abelian subvarieties of a principally polarized
abelian variety have the same exponent.
Note that for an arbitrary polarization Corollary 5.3.2 is not valid. For an example,
see Exercise 5.3.4 (1).

5.3.3 Topological Construction of Prym Varieties
In Theorem 5.3.9 we saw that there are two types of double coverings determining
Prym varieties: namely those ramified in none or two points. In this section we study
these coverings from the topological point of view. This gives a second proof of the
fact that they determine Prym varieties.

Remark 5.3.13 Every Jacobian (ùêΩ, Œò) of a smooth projective curve ùê∂of genus ùëî
is a limit of a family of Prym varieties (ùëÉùë°, Œûùë°) of dimension ùëîassociated to √©tale
double coverings.

5.3.4 Exercises and Further Results
(1) Give an example of a pair of complementary abelian subvarieties of a non-
principally polarized abelian variety for which Corollary 5.3.2 is not valid.
(Hint: Consider a product of polarized abelian varieties with polarizations of
suitable different types.)
(2) Suppose (ùëã, ùêø) is a polarized abelian variety of dimension ùëî.
(a) Show that for any positive integer ùëõthere are only finitely many abelian
subvarieties of ùëãof exponent less than or equal to ùëõ.
(b) Conclude that for any smooth projective curve ùê∂and any positive integer ùëõ
there are up to isomorphism only finitely many morphisms of degree less
than or equal to ùëõof ùê∂onto curves of genus ‚â•1.
A special case of (b) is the Theorem of de Franchis [44]: any smooth projective
curve ùê∂admits up to isomorphism only finitely many morphisms onto curves
of genus ‚â•2.

5.4 Intermediate Jacobians
In Chapter 4 we associated to every smooth projective curve ùê∂an abelian variety
which reflects its geometry, the Jacobian ùêΩ= ùêΩ(ùê∂). If ùëÄis a smooth projective
variety of dimension ùëõ> 1, there are several ways to associate to ùëÄan abelian
variety with some geometric relevance: In Section 5.2 we defined the Picard variety
ùêΩ1 := Pic0(ùëÄ) using the first cohomology of ùëÄand the Albanese variety ùêΩùëõ:=
Alb(ùëÄ) using the (2ùëõ‚àí1)-th cohomology of ùëÄ(see Section 5.4.2 below), which
is dual to ùêΩ1. So for ùëõ= 1, ùêΩ= ùêΩ1 is self-dual. For ùëõ> 2, every cohomology of odd
weight yields an abelian variety. These are the Intermediate Jacobians ùêΩùëù(ùëÄ). They
are in general highly decomposable complex tori. This implies that there are many
different possibilities for their definition.

called the p-th Weil Intermediate Jacobian (see Section 5.4.3). It has the disadvantage

5.4.3 The Weil Intermediate Jacobian

abelian variety of the same dimension as the corresponding Griffiths Intermediate
Jacobian.

5.4.4 The Lazzeri Intermediate Jacobian
The Lazzeri Intermediate Jacobian was studied by E. Rubei in her thesis [113]. It
can be defined more generally for every oriented Riemannian manifold ùëÄwhose
(real) dimension is twice an odd prime number.

5.4.5 The Abelian Variety Associated to the Griffiths Intermediate
Jacobian
Let ùëÄbe a smooth projective variety of dimension ùëõ. In this section we show that

canonical way. If moreover ùëõ= 2ùëù‚àí1 this abelian variety coincides with the Lazzeri
Intermediate Jacobian.

Chapter 6
The Fourier Transform for Sheaves and Cycles
In the theory of algebraic cycles it was quite common to use the Poincar√© bundle to
transfer cycles on an abelian variety ùëãto cycles on the dual abelian variety (see for
example Weil [137]). To be more precise: If ùëéis a cycle class on ùëã, Pùëãdenotes the
Poincar√© bundle on ùëã√ó bùëã, and ùëù1 and ùëù2 are the projections of ùëã√ó bùëã, then
ùêπùëã(ùëé) := ùëù2‚àó
 ùëê1[P] ¬∑ ùëù‚àó
1ùëé
is a cycle class on bùëã.
It was the fundamental idea of Mukai [95] to apply the same construction for
sheaves. Similarly, if F is a coherent sheaf on ùëã, then
ùêπùëã(F ) := ùëù2‚àó(Pùëã‚äóùëù‚àó
1F )
is a coherent sheaf on bùëã. In general this sheaf is not very useful. However, if F is a
WIT-sheaf, meaning that ùëÖùëóùëù2‚àó(Pùëã‚äóùëù‚àó
1F ) = 0 for all ùëó‚â†ùëñ. then the sheaf
bF := ùëÖùëñùëù2‚àó(Pùëã‚äóùëù‚àó
1F )
is an important coherent sheaf on bùëã, called the Fourier‚ÄìMukai transform of F . Mukai
defines the transform more generally for all complexes of Oùëã-modules with bounded
coherent cohomology in the corresponding derived category. For this, see Remark
6.1.18. The first section gives details on WIT-sheaves and some consequences on
the theory of vector bundles on abelian varieties.
For abelian varieties one knows a little more about algebraic cycles than for most
other classes of smooth projective varieties. This is mainly due to the fact that the
Chow group Ch(ùëã) admits two ring structures, one is induced by the intersection
product and the other by the Pontryagin product. The Fourier transform exchanges
both ring structures. In the second section we give an introduction into the theory
of algebraic cycles on abelian varieties. In order to make this as self-contained as
possible we introduce the Chow ring Ch‚Ä¢(ùëã) in Section 6.2.1 and prove the main
properties of correspondences which are needed in Section 6.2.2.

6.1.2 WIT-sheaves
Let ùëãbe an abelian variety. Recall that the index ùëñ(ùêø) of a non-degenerate line
bundle ùêøon ùëãis the number of negative eigenvalues of its associated hermitian
form. Mumford‚Äôs Index Theorem 1.6.5 says: For any non-degenerate ùêø‚ààPic(ùëã),
ùêªùëó(ùêø‚äóùëÉ) = 0
for all
ùëÉ‚ààPic0(ùëã)
and
ùëó‚â†ùëñ(ùêø).
Induced by this, Mukai called more generally any coherent sheaf F on ùëãan IT-sheaf
of index ùëñ(IT stands for Index Theorem) if
ùêªùëó(F ‚äóùëÉ) = 0
for all
ùëÉ‚ààPic0(ùëã)
and
ùëó‚â†ùëñ.
The following lemma might be called the Weak Index Lemma.
Lemma 6.1.9 Let F be an IT-sheaf of index ùëñon ùëã. Then
(a) ùëÖùëóùëù2‚àó(Pùëã‚äóùëù‚àó
1F ) = 0 for ùëó‚â†ùëñ,

1F ) is locally free of finite rank on bùëã.

 = 0
for ùëó‚â†ùëñ. Now the assertion follows from the Base Change Theorem (see Hartshorne

is called the Fourier or Fourier‚ÄìMukai transform of F . With this terminology
Lemma 6.1.9 implies that every IT-sheaf of index ùëñis a WIT-sheaf of the same index.
In particular, a non-degenerate line bundle ùêøof index ùëñis a WIT-sheaf of index ùëñand
its Fourier transform bùêøis a vector bundle on bùëã.

Remark 6.1.18 There is a generalization of the Fourier‚ÄìMukai functor to all coherent

6.2 The Fourier Transform on the Chow and Cohomology Rings
There is an analogue of the Fourier‚ÄìMukai transform on Chow rings and cohomology
rings which is introduced in this section. The first section contains the definition of
the Chow group of a smooth projective variety and some of its properties. The second
section gives a generalization of correspondences between two curves, as defined in
Section 4.6.1, to arbitrary smooth projective varieties. After that we are in a position
to define and study the Fourier transform on the Chow ring and the cohomology ring
of a smooth projective variety.
6.2.1 Chow Groups
In this section we compile some generalities about algebraic cycles and Chow groups.
For more details and proofs we refer to the standard books on intersection theory
(preferably Fulton [47]).
Let ùëãbe a smooth projective variety of dimension ùëîover the field of complex
numbers. Denote by Zùëò(ùëã) the free abelian group generated by all subvarieties of

subvarieties Vùëñ‚äÇùëãof dimension ùëò. They are called algebraic cycles of dimension

6.2.2 Correspondences
In Section 4.6.1 we defined a correspondence between two smooth projective curves
ùê∂1 and ùê∂2 to be a line bundle on ùê∂1 √ó ùê∂2. According to Theorem 4.6.1 any such
correspondence induces a homomorphism Pic0(ùê∂1) ‚ÜíPic0(ùê∂2). By Remark 6.2.3
we have Pic(ùê∂ùúà) = Ch1(ùê∂ùúà). More generally, let ùëã1 and ùëã2 be any smooth projective
varieties. In this section we will see that any cycle on ùëã1√óùëã2 induces homomorphisms
between the Chow groups of ùëã1 and ùëã2.

6.2.3 The Fourier Transform on the Chow Ring
The Fourier transform on the level of cycles has been thoroughly investigated by
Beauville in [15]. In this section we give the definition of the Fourier transform on
the Chow ring and derive some properties. In particular it exchanges, up to sign, the
intersection product by the Pontryagin product.
Let ùëãbe an abelian variety of dimension ùëî. The Fourier functor ùêπ= ùêπùëãis defined
on the Chow ring with Q-coefficients:
Chùëù(ùëã)Q := Chùëù(ùëã) ‚äóZ Q
and
Ch(ùëã)Q := Ch(ùëã) ‚äóZ Q.
All definitions and properties of Ch(ùëã) of Sections 6.2.1 and 6.2.2 extend to Ch(ùëã)Q
in an obvious way.
Consider the Poincar√© bundle P = Pùëã‚ààPic(ùëã√ó bùëã) = Ch1(ùëã√ó bùëã) (see Sections
1.4.4 and 6.1.1). We denote its image in Ch1(ùëã√ó bùëã)Q by the same letter. The
correspondence
eP :=
‚àëÔ∏Å
ùúà‚â•0
1
ùúà!P¬∑ùúà‚ààCh(ùëã√ó bùëã)Q
is well-defined, the sum being finite. The correspondence eP defines a homomor-
phism of groups
ùêπ= ùêπùëã: Ch(ùëã)Q ‚ÜíCh( bùëã)Q,
ùõº‚Ü¶‚ÜíeP(ùõº) = ùëù2‚àó(eP ¬∑ùëù‚àó
1ùõº),
called the Fourier transform on Ch(ùëã)Q.

Remark 6.2.13 Note that according to Fulton [47], eP coincides with the Chern

Mukai transform of a WIT-sheaf, associating to a WIT-sheaf on ùëãa WIT-sheaf on

above Fourier transform on Ch(ùëã)Q.

Dùëè(ùëã) of Oùëã-modules on ùëã(which we do not study in this book, but mentioned

6.2.5 Exercises
Let Z ùëù
alg(ùëã) denote the subgroup of Z ùëù(ùëã) generated by all cycles of the form
ùëç(ùëù1) ‚àíùëç(ùëù2), where ùëç‚ààZ ùëù(ùê∂√ó ùëã) with a smooth projective curve ùê∂, and

6.3 Some Results on the Chow Ring of an Abelian Variety
In the first section we prove an eigenspace decomposition of the Chow ring of an
abelian variety, due to Beauville [16]. A consequence is a generalization of the
Poincar√© Formula 4.2.2 to an arbitrary polarized abelian variety, given in Section
6.3.2. Finally, in the last two sections some results on the K√ºnneth decomposition
of the Chow ring of ùëã√ó ùëãare given, mainly due to Deninger‚ÄìMurre [38] and
K√ºnnemann [78]. The most important result is the decomposition of the diagonal Œî
in Chùëî(ùëã√ó ùëã)Q.

Chapter 7
Introduction to the Hodge Conjecture for
Abelian Varieties
The original version of the conjecture was first formulated by Hodge in his 1950
ICM congress address [64]. In 1961 Atiyah and Hirzebruch formulated a modified
version of it in [11], which since then has been considered as the Hodge conjecture.
Although the Hodge conjecture is formulated for arbitrary smooth complex pro-
jective varieties, abelian varieties are considered as a test for its validity. In this
chapter we give an introduction to this subject.
There are numerous examples of abelian varieties for which the Hodge conjecture
has been proven. Here we outline the proof only in the two simplest cases, namely
for general polarized abelian varieties and for general Jacobian varieties. For these
the proof is particularly simple, and moreover uses the method which is applied in
almost all other cases. For a few more examples, see Sections 7.2.4 and 7.3.3.
A class in ùêª2ùëù(ùëã, Q) is called algebraic if it is in the image of the cycle map
cl : ùê∂ùêªùëù(ùëã)Q ‚Üíùêª2ùëù(ùëã, Q) of Chapter 6. On the other hand, the elements of the
subvector space
ùêª2ùëù
Hodge(ùëã) := ùêª2ùëù(ùëã, Q) ‚à©ùêªùëù,ùëù(ùëã)
are called Hodge classes. According to Lemma 6.2.7 every algebraic class is a Hodge
class. The Hodge (ùëù, ùëù)-conjecture asserts the converse, i.e.,
cl Chùëù(ùëã)Q
 = ùêª2ùëù
Hodge(ùëã).
The proof of the Hodge (ùëù, ùëù)-conjecture for a variety ùëãconsists of two steps:
(1) Determine the vector subspace of Hodge classes ùêª2ùëù
Hodge(ùëã).
(2) Verify that every element of ùêª2ùëù
Hodge(ùëã) is algebraic.
Both steps are difficult in general. In the case of abelian varieties Mumford had
for step (1) the fundamental idea to realize ùêª2ùëù
Hodge(ùëã) as the space of invariants
under a certain reductive group, the Hodge-group, sometimes also called the special
Mumford‚ÄìTate group. This reduces step (1) to classical invariant theory. For general
347
¬© The Author(s), under exclusive license to Springer Nature Switzerland AG 2023 
H. Lange, Abelian Varieties over the Complex Numbers, Grundlehren Text Editions, 
https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-25570-0_7

348
7 Introduction to the Hodge Conjecture for Abelian Varieties
polarized abelian varieties and general Jacobians step (2) is particularly easy, due to
the fact that for these varieties the ring of Hodge classes ùêª2‚Ä¢
Hodge(ùëã) is generated by
classes of divisors.
In the first section we introduce Hodge structures and study their relation to
complex structures. The aim is to express the Siegel upper half space ‚Ñåùëîin terms
of symplectic complex structures. In Section 7.2 the Hodge group is introduced and
shown to be reductive. The third section contains the proof of a theorem of Mattuck,
saying that for a general polarized abelian variety (ùëã, ùêø) the Hodge ring ùêª2‚Ä¢(ùëã) is
generated by the class of ùêø, which easily implies the Hodge conjecture. The proof
of the Hodge conjecture for general Jacobian varieties is a special case of a more
general result of Biswas‚ÄìNarasimhan [25], who showed an analogous conjecture for
certain moduli spaces of stable parabolic vector bundles. Finally, in the last section
some more special results on the conjecture are quoted in order to induce the reader
to further studies.
In this chapter we apply some basic facts for algebraic and Lie groups, mainly in
the case of the symplectic group. Moreover, we use the following notation: If ùëâis
a ùêæ-vector space and ùê¥a ùêæ-algebra we denote ùëâ‚äóùêæùê¥by ùëâùê¥. If ùê∫is an algebraic
group defined over ùêæ, ùê∫(ùê¥) denotes the group of ùê¥-valued points of the algebraic
variety ùê∫.
7.1 Complex Structures
In Section 7.1.1 we study the relation of Hodge structures and complex structures
and use this in Section 7.1.2 to express the Siegel upper half plane Hùëîin terms of
symplectic complex structures.
7.1.1 Hodge Structures and Complex Structures
Let ùëâbe a real vector space of finite dimension and ùëõa nonzero integer. A Hodge
structure of weight ùëõon ùëâis by definition a decomposition
ùëâC = √â
ùëù+ùëû=ùëõùëâùëù,ùëû
with complex subvector spaces ùëâùëù,ùëûsatisfying
ùëâùëù,ùëû= ùëâùëû,ùëù
for all ùëù, ùëûwith ùëù+ùëû= ùëõ. In the same way one defines Hodge structures on Q-vector
spaces, which sometimes are also called rational Hodge structures. In this section
we consider Hodge structures of weight ‚àí1. In particular we will see that Hodge
structures of weight ‚àí1 are equivalent to complex structures. So Hodge structures
may be thought of as generalizations of complex structures.

7.1 Complex Structures
349

ùë•ùë£+ ùë¶ùêΩ(ùë£). In this chapter we denote by S1 the circle group

7.1.2 Symplectic Complex Structures
In this section we give a description of the Siegel upper half space Hùëîin terms of
complex structures.
As in the last section let ùëâbe a real vector space of dimension 2ùëî. Fix a lattice
Œõ ‚äÜùëâand a non-degenerate alternating form ùê∏: ùëâ√óùëâ‚àí‚ÜíR taking integer values
on Œõ. As usual denote by Sp(ùëâ, ùê∏) the symplectic group associated to ùê∏. A complex
structure ùêΩon ùëâcontained in Sp(ùëâ, ùê∏) is called a symplectic complex structure.

7.2.3 Reductivity of the Hodge Group and a Criterion for its
Commutativity
The following proposition gives a criterion for Hg(ùëã) to be an abelian group.
Proposition 7.2.6 For an abelian variety ùëãof dimension ùëîthe following conditions
are equivalent:
(i) the Hodge group Hg(ùëã) is commutative;
(ii) EndQ(ùëã) contains a commutative semisimple Q-algebra of dimension 2ùëî.
This applies for example to abelian varieties of CM-type in the sense of Exercise
2.6.3 (4). Slightly more generally, abelian varieties satisfying condition (ii) are also
sometimes said to be of CM-type.
Proof (i)‚áí(ii): Suppose Hg(ùëã) is commutative. Since Hg(ùëã) is connected it is
contained in a maximal torus ùëá‚äÇGL(ùëâ) ‚äÇEnd(ùëâ). So ùëá‚äÇEnd(ùëâ)Hg(ùëã) =
EndQ(ùëã) by Proposition 7.2.5. So EndQ(ùëã) satisfies condition (ii), since [ùëá: Q] =
dimQ ùëâ= 2 dim ùëã.
(ii) ‚áí(i): Let ùëá‚äÇEndQ(ùëã) be a commutative semisimple Q-algebra of rank 2ùëî
over Q. Consider ùëáas a maximal commutative semisimple subalgebra of End(ùëâ)
via the rational representation. According to Proposition 7.2.5 the Hodge group
Hg(ùëã) ‚äÇEnd(ùëâ) commutes with ùëá. By the maximality of ùëá, the centralizer of ùëáis
ùëáitself. Hence Hg(ùëã) is contained in ùëáand thus Hg(ùëã) is also commutative.
‚ñ°
Recall that a linear algebraic group ùê∫defined over a field ùêæof characteristic 0
is called reductive if every ùêæ-representation of ùê∫is fully reducible. Equivalent to
this is that there are normal subgroups ùê∫ùëéand ùê∫ùë†of ùê∫defined over ùêæsuch that ùê∫ùëé
is a torus, ùê∫ùë†is semi-simple (meaning that ùê∫ùë†(ùêæ) is semi-simple) and ùê∫(ùêæ) is an
almost direct product of ùê∫ùëé(ùêæ) and ùê∫ùë†(ùêæ) (that is, ùê∫ùëé(ùêæ) ¬∑ùê∫ùë†(ùêæ) is Zariski-dense
in ùê∫(ùêæ) and ùê∫ùëé(ùêæ) ‚à©ùê∫ùë†(ùêæ) is finite). Note that ùê∫is reductive if and only if the
scalar extension ùê∫ùêøis reductive for some field extension ùêø|ùêæ(see Satake [115,
Section I.3]).
The following proposition, due to Mumford [99], is important for applying in-
variant theoretical results.
Proposition 7.2.7 Hg(ùëã) is a reductive group.
Proof As mentioned above, the group Hg(ùëã), defined over Q, is reductive if and
only if Hg(ùëã)ùêøis reductive for some field extension ùêøof Q. Hence the group Hg(ùëã)

Consider the complex structure ùêΩ= ‚Ñé(ùëñ) as an element of Hg(ùëã)R. The inner

7.3 The Hodge Conjecture for General Abelian and Jacobian Varieties
361
7.3 The Hodge Conjecture for General Abelian and Jacobian
Varieties
Section 7.3.1 contains the proof of a theorem of Mattuck [92] of which the Hodge
conjecture for a general polarized abelian variety is an easy consequence. In Section
7.3.2 a proof of the Hodge conjecture for a general Jacobian variety is given. It is a
special case of a more general result of Biswas and Narasimhan [25]. Finally, in the
last section, apart from a few exercises, we quote without proof several results on
the Hodge conjecture for special abelian varieties.
7.3.1 The Theorem of Mattuck
Let ùëãbe an abelian variety. The images of algebraic cycles under the cycle map
cl : Ch‚Ä¢(ùëã)Q ‚àí‚Üíùêª2‚Ä¢(ùëã, Q) are called algebraic classes. Every algebraic class
is a Q-linear combination of fundamental classes of algebraic subvarieties of ùëã.
According to Lemma 6.2.7 every algebraic class is a Hodge class, that is,
cl(Ch‚Ä¢(ùëã)Q) ‚äÜùêª2‚Ä¢
Hodge(ùëã).
The Hodge conjecture asserts that even equality holds. For fixed codimension ùëùthe
statement
cl(Chùëù(ùëã)Q) = ùêª2ùëù
Hodge(ùëã)
is called the Hodge (ùëù, ùëù)-conjecture.
According to Remark 6.2.3 and for trivial reasons the Hodge (ùëù, ùëù)-conjecture
holds for ùëù= 0 and 1. The aim of this section is to show that for general polarized
abelian varieties the Hodge (ùëù, ùëù)-conjecture holds for all ùëù. Here general means
an abelian variety outside the union of countably many proper analytic subvarieties
of the moduli space of polarized abelian varieties of a fixed type (see the proof of
Proposition 7.3.2).
Denote by ùê∑‚Ä¢ = ùê∑‚Ä¢(ùëã) the subring of ùêª2‚Ä¢
Hodge(ùëã) = √â
ùëùùêª2ùëù
Hodge(ùëã) generated
by ùêª0
Hodge(ùëã) and ùêª2
Hodge(ùëã). By what we have said above the cycle classes in ùê∑‚Ä¢
are all algebraic. Hence the Hodge (ùëù, ùëù)-conjecture is true if
ùê∑ùëù= ùêª2ùëù
Hodge(ùëã).
The following theorem of Mattuck [92] implies the Hodge (ùëù, ùëù)-conjecture for a
general polarized abelian variety.
Theorem 7.3.1 For a general polarized abelian variety (ùëã, ùê∏) of dimension ùëîand
all ùëù= 0, . . . , ùëî,

7.3.2 The Hodge Conjecture for a General Jacobian
In this section we prove the Hodge (ùëù, ùëù)-conjecture for a general Jacobian. The
notion of a general Jacobian is defined in the same way as the notion of a general
polarized abelian variety. As we saw in Remark 4.7.5, Torelli‚Äôs Theorem 4.3.1 implies
that under the map Mùëî‚ÜíA1g the moduli space of Jacobians of a fixed dimension
maps bijectively to an irreducible locally closed subvariety of the same dimension
of the corresponding moduli space of principally polarized abelian varieties. Hence
a general Jacobian means a Jacobian outside countable many proper subvarieties.
The idea is to use a universal curve and its associated family of Jacobians. As
in the previous section the Hodge (ùëù, ùëù)-conjecture is a direct consequence of the
following theorem.
Theorem 7.3.4 For a general polarized Jacobian (ùêΩ(ùê∂), Œò) of dimension ùëîand all
ùëù= 0, . . . , ùëî

Hodge(ùêΩ(ùê∂)) = ùê∑ùëù(ùêΩ(ùê∂)) ‚âÉQ.

Corollary 7.3.5 The N√©ron‚ÄìSeveri group of a general polarized Jacobian (ùêΩ(ùê∂), Œò)

7.3 The Hodge Conjecture for General Abelian and Jacobian Varieties
367
7.3.3 Exercises and Further Results
Although the Hodge conjecture is true for general polarized abelian and Jacobian
varieties, there are many families of abelian varieties where the conjecture is open.
To the best of my knowledge we owe this insight to Weil [141]. In this section some
special results on the subject are given with a reference, but without proof. The first
two exercises should be solved by everybody.
(1) Let ùëì: ùëã‚Üíùëåbe an isogeny of abelian varieties. Show that

abelian variety.

dimension ùëõ.
